Contradictions of Compound Modal Propositions
Author lists contradictions of seven compound modal types and explains how to derive them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any given compound modal proposition by identifying its constituent simple propositions and forming the appropriate exclusive alternative disjunction.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound modal is an exclusive alternative disjunction of its opposing simples.

In the previous lesson, you learned about the contradictions of simple modal propositions. Now, the author shows how to extend that to compound modal propositions. By understanding the internal structure of each compound type, you can derive its contradictory by listing the two conflicting simple propositions and forming a disjunction that excludes both being false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound type
Recognize which named compound modal is given (e.g., mashrūṭa khāṣṣa, 'ārifiyya khāṣṣa, waqtiyya, muntashira, wujūdiyya lā-dā'ima, wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya, mumkina khāṣṣa).
These are listed in the passage.
2
Recall the constituent simples
Each compound is built from two simple modal propositions that differ in quality or quantity. For example, mashrūṭa khāṣṣa combines ḥīniyya mumkina and dā'ima differing in quality.
The passage specifies the constituents for each type.
3
Form the contradictory disjunction
The contradictory is an exclusive alternative disjunction (māni‘a al-khulū) of the two constituent simples. Use the two simples as the disjuncts.
The disjunction must be exclusive, meaning exactly one is true.
4
Apply to particulars
For particular compound propositions, analogize, but replace the disjunction with a categorical proposition that has a disjunctive predicate, keeping the same modalities.
The passage explicitly states this adaptation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ اطِّلَاعِكَ عَلَى حَقَائِقِ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ في مبحث الموجهات (ونقائض الْبَسَائِطِ) ههنا (تَتَمَكَّنُ مِنِ اسْتِخْرَاجِ التَّفَاصِيلِ)، أي تفاصيل النقائض، فنقيض المشروطة الخاصة المنفصلة المانعة الخلو، المركبة من الحينية الممكنة والدائمة المتخالفتين كيفا، والعرفية الخاصة المركبة من الحينية المطلقة والدائمة المتخالفتين، والوقتية المركبة من الوقتية الممكنة والدائمة المتخالفتين، والمنتشرة المركبة من الممكنة الدائمة والدائمة المطلقة المتخالفتين، والوجودية اللادائمة المركبة من الدائمتين المتخالفتين، والوجودية اللاضرورية المركبة من الدائمة والضرورية المتخالفتين، والممكنة الخاصة المركبة من الضروريتين المتخالفتين . وهذا في الكليات، وأما الجزئيات فقس عليها، وخذ مكان المنفصل الحملية المرددة المحمول، واحفظ الجهات المذكورات بعينها في المحمول. ولما فرغ عن نقائض الحمليات شرع في نقائض الشرطيات، وقال: (وَفِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِلَافِ كَيْفًا وَكَمَّا يَجِبُ الاتِّحَادُ فِي الْجِنْسِ)، أي الاتصال والانفصال، فنقيض المتصلة متصلة، ونقيض المنفصلة منفصلة، (وَالنَّوْع) ، أي اللزوم والعناد والاتفاق ، فنقيض اللزومية لزومية، والعنادية عنادية ، والاتفاقية اتفاقية، ومن تذكر مفاهيم هذه القضايا لا يخفى عليه ذلك، (فَافْهَمْ). فيه إشارة إلى أنه إنما يجب في النقيض الصريح ، وإلا فقد سبق أن المركبة الكلية نقيضها مانعة الخلو، والتناقض من الطرفين، فتلك الكلية نقيض هذه المانعة الخلو التي هي شرطية ، كذا في الحاشية». هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
And after your awareness of the realities of the compound [propositions] in the section on modalities, and the contradictions of simple [propositions] here, you will be able to extract the details, i.e., the details of the contradictions. So the contradictory of the particular mashrūṭa [conditional] is the exclusive alternative disjunction composed of the possible ḥīniyya and the perpetual [dā'ima] which differ in quality; and the contradictory of the particular 'ārifiyya is [a disjunction] composed of the absolute ḥīniyya and the perpetual which differ; and the contradictory of the waqtiyya is composed of the possible waqtiyya and the perpetual which differ; and the contradictory of the muntashira is composed of the possible perpetual and the absolute perpetual which differ; and the contradictory of the wujūdiyya lā-dā'ima is composed of the two perpetuals which differ; and the contradictory of the wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya is composed of the perpetual and the necessary which differ; and the contradictory of the possible khāṣṣa is composed of the two necessary [propositions] which differ. This is for universals; as for particulars, analogize to it, and replace the disjunction with a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate, and keep the same modalities in the predicate. When he finished with the contradictions of categoricals, he began with the contradictions of conditionals, and said: 'In conditionals, after difference in quality and quantity, unity in genus is required,' i.e., in conjunction and disjunction. So the contradictory of a conjunctive is conjunctive, and of a disjunctive is disjunctive. 'And also in species,' i.e., in implication (luzūm), incompatibility (ʿinād), and coincidence (itifāq). So the contradictory of an implicative is implicative, of an incompatibility is incompatibility, and of a coincidental is coincidental. Whoever recalls the concepts of these propositions will not be unaware of that. 'So understand.' In it is an indication that this is only required for the explicit contradictory; otherwise, it has preceded that the universal compound [proposition] has as contradictory an exclusive alternative, and the contradiction is from both sides, so that universal [proposition] is the contradictory of this exclusive alternative which is a conditional. This is in the gloss. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage summarizes the application of earlier principles to various compound modal propositions, listing each type (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa, 'ārifiyya khāṣṣa, waqtiyya, muntashira, wujūdiyya lā-dā'ima, wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya, mumkina khāṣṣa) and their contradictions as exclusive alternative disjunctions. Then for conditionals, it specifies the additional requirements of unity in genus (conjunctive/disjunctive) and species (implication/incompatibility/coincidence). The final note reiterates that this only applies to the explicit contradictory, referencing the earlier exception.

A) An exclusive disjunction of the possible ḥīniyya and the perpetual differing in quality.
B) A universal affirmative categorical proposition.

D) A conjunctive conditional with the same modalities.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition is an exclusive disjunction of its two opposing simple propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the compound modal proposition of type wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya (existential non-necessary), which according to the passage is composed of a perpetual and a necessary proposition differing in quality, write its contradictory as an exclusive alternative disjunction using Arabic logical terms.
?
Take a particular instance of the mashrūṭa khāṣṣa type (e.g., 'Some A are B when C is the case') and construct its contradictory as a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate, following the rule for particulars.

Rules for Contradiction in Conditional Propositions
Author sets the conditions for contradiction of conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two conditional propositions are contradictories by checking unity in genus (conjunctive/disjunctive) and species (implication/incompatibility/coincidence) and applying the rules from the passage.

Core Idea
A conditional and its contradictory must share the same genus and species.

Having covered contradictions of categorical compounds, the author now turns to conditionals. He lays down a crucial rule: for a conditional to be the contradictory of another, they must agree in both genus (conjunctive or disjunctive) and species (implication, incompatibility, or coincidence). This prevents false contradictions and ensures logical precision.

Guided Path
1
Determine the genus
Is the conditional conjunctive (muttaṣila) or disjunctive (munfaṣila)? This is the first requirement for a valid contradiction.
The passage says: 'فنقيض المتصلة متصلة، ونقيض المنفصلة منفصلة'.
2
Determine the species
Is it implicative (luzūmiyya), incompatibility ('inādiyya), or coincidence (itifāqiyya)? The contradictory must share the same species.
The passage says: 'فنقيض اللزومية لزومية، والعنادية عنادية، والاتفاقية اتفاقية'.
3
Verify quality and quantity
The contradictory must also differ in quality and quantity as per the general rules for simple propositions. The passage mentions 'after difference in quality and quantity'.
This is in addition to genus and species unity.
4
Understand the exception
The rule applies only to explicit contradictories. For universal compound propositions, their contradictory might be an exclusive alternative disjunction as mentioned earlier.
The passage references a gloss about this exception.

What two things must be the same between a conditional proposition and its contradictory, according to the passage?
A) The subject and predicate terms.
B) The genus (conjunctive/disjunctive) and species (implication/incompatibility/coincidence).
C) The modality and quantity.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the conditional proposition: 'If the sun has risen, then it is daytime' (a conjunctive implicative), construct its proper contradictory. Explain why 'If the sun has risen, then it is not daytime' is the correct form, while 'If the sun has risen, it is not daytime' is a disjunctive conditional that does not meet the genus requirement.
?
Identify the genus and species of the following conditional: 'Either the prayer is valid or the ablution is invalid.' (It is a disjunctive incompatibility conditional.) Then, state what its contradictory must be in terms of genus and species.

Finding Contradictories of Compound Modal Propositions
Author explains how to find contradictories of compound modal categorical and conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of a given compound modal categorical or conditional proposition by applying the rules of quality, quantity, and modal composition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound mirrors its structure while inverting quality and preserving quantity.

In Islamic logic, mastering contradiction is essential for valid argumentation. This lesson unpacks the rules for finding contradictories of complex modal propositions, both categorical and conditional. By understanding these rules, you can evaluate whether two propositions truly oppose each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (if-then or either-or), and whether it is simple or compound. Also note the modal aspects (temporal, perpetual, necessary, possible).
The compound modal categoricals mentioned are composed of two simple modals, e.g., possible temporal and perpetual.
2
For categorical: extract the two component propositions
A compound modal categorical like 'all A are B possibly at time t and always' has two parts: one with a temporal modality and one with a perpetual modality, differing in quality. The contradictory combines these parts with a disjunction (or conjunction) that covers all individuals.
The author lists several types: mashruta khassa, 'urfiyya khassa, waqtiyya, muntashira, etc.
3
For conditionals: ensure genus and species match
The contradictory of a conjunctive conditional must be conjunctive; the contradictory of a disjunctive must be disjunctive. Within each, the species (implication, opposition, coincidence) must be preserved. Only quality and quantity are changed.
Implicative's contradictory is implicative, etc.
4
Verify the contradictory conditions
Check that the contradictory differs in quality (affirmative vs negative) and quantity (universal vs particular) while maintaining identity in subject, predicate, time, and (for conditionals) the connective type and species.
For universal compound categoricals, the contradictory is a particular exclusive disjunctive (mani'a al-khuluww), which is itself a conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التناقض
إِلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ مِنَ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، فَهِيَ قَضِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ مَرْدُودَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ) بين السلب والإيجاب، فالنقيض في المثال المذكور كل واحد واحد من أفراد الحيوان إما إنسان دائما أو ليس بإنسان دائما» ، وهو صادق.
(وَبَعْدَ اطَّلَاعِكَ عَلَى حَقَائِقِ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ في مبحث الموجهات (ونقائض الْبَسَائِطِ) ههنا (تَتَمَكَّنُ مِنِ اسْتِخْرَاجِ التَّفَاصِيلِ)، أي تفاصيل النقائض، فنقيض المشروطة الخاصة المنفصلة المانعة الخلو، المركبة من الحينية الممكنة والدائمة المتخالفتين كيفا، والعرفية الخاصة المركبة من الحينية المطلقة والدائمة المتخالفتين، والوقتية المركبة من الوقتية الممكنة والدائمة المتخالفتين، والمنتشرة المركبة من الممكنة الدائمة والدائمة المطلقة المتخالفتين، والوجودية اللادائمة المركبة من الدائمتين المتخالفتين، والوجودية اللاضرورية المركبة من الدائمة والضرورية المتخالفتين، والممكنة الخاصة المركبة من الضروريتين المتخالفتين .
وهذا في الكليات، وأما الجزئيات فقس عليها، وخذ مكان المنفصل الحملية المرددة المحمول، واحفظ الجهات المذكورات بعينها في المحمول. ولما فرغ عن نقائض الحمليات شرع في نقائض الشرطيات، وقال: (وَفِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِلَافِ كَيْفًا وَكَمَّا يَجِبُ الاتِّحَادُ فِي الْجِنْسِ)، أي الاتصال والانفصال، فنقيض المتصلة متصلة، ونقيض المنفصلة منفصلة، (وَالنَّوْع) ، أي اللزوم والعناد والاتفاق ، فنقيض اللزومية لزومية، والعنادية عنادية ، والاتفاقية اتفاقية، ومن تذكر مفاهيم هذه القضايا لا يخفى عليه ذلك، (فَافْهَمْ). فيه إشارة إلى أنه إنما يجب في النقيض الصريح ، وإلا فقد سبق أن المركبة الكلية نقيضها مانعة الخلو، والتناقض من الطرفين، فتلك الكلية نقيض هذه المانعة الخلو التي هي شرطية ، كذا في الحاشية». هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Chapter on Contradiction. To every individual of the subject, so it is a categorical proposition with a repeated predicate (between negation and affirmation). So the contradictory in the mentioned example: every single individual of animal is either always human or always not human, and it is true. And after your knowledge of the realities of compounds in the topic of modal propositions and the contradictories of simples here, you become able to extract the details, i.e., the details of contradictories. So the contradictory of the particular conditioned disjunctive proposition that prevents emptiness, composed of the possible temporal and the perpetual differing in quality, and the particular conventional composed of the absolute temporal and the perpetual differing, and the temporal composed of the possible temporal and the perpetual differing, and the distributive composed of the perpetual possible and the absolute perpetual differing, and the existential non-perpetual composed of the two perpetuals differing, and the existential non-necessary composed of the perpetual and the necessary differing, and the particular possible composed of the two necessaries differing. And this is for universals; as for particulars, analogize to them, and take in place of the disjunctive the categorical with repeated predicate, and preserve the modes mentioned exactly in the predicate. And when he finished with contradictories of categoricals, he began on contradictories of conditionals, and said: 'And in conditionals, after differing in quality and quantity, unity in genus is required,' i.e., conjunction and disjunction, so the contradictory of a conjunctive is conjunctive, and the contradictory of a disjunctive is disjunctive, 'and the species,' i.e., implication, opposition, and coincidence, so the contradictory of an implicative is implicative, of an oppositive is oppositive, and of a coincidental is coincidental. And one who recalls the concepts of these propositions will not be unaware of that. 'So understand.' In it is an indication that this is only required in the explicit contradictory; otherwise, it has preceded that the universal compound's contradictory is the exclusive particular, and contradiction is from both sides, so that universal is the contradictory of this exclusive particular which is a conditional, as in the gloss. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of how to find the contradictory (naqid) of various types of propositions, particularly modal and compound ones. The author begins by illustrating a contradictory in a simple categorical proposition: for each individual animal, it is either always human or always not human. This is a true contradictory because it covers every individual with exhaustive and exclusive alternatives. Then he states that once you understand the reality of compound modal propositions and the contradictories of simple ones, you can derive the contradictories of complex ones. He lists several types: the particular conditioned disjunctive (al-mashruta al-khassa al-munfasila) that excludes emptiness, composed of a possible temporal (hiniyya mumkina) and a perpetual (da'ima) differing in quality; similarly the particular conventional ('urfiyya khassa), temporal (waqtiyya), distributive (muntashira), existential non-perpetual (wujudiyya la-da'ima), existential non-necessary (wujudiyya la-daruriyya), and particular possible (mumkina khassa). All these are composed of two propositions differing in quality. This is for universal propositions; for particulars, you analogize, replacing the disjunction with a categorical with a repeated predicate (marduda al-mahmul), preserving the modes. Then he turns to conditionals (shartiyyat), citing a statement: in conditionals, after ensuring difference in quality and quantity, unity is required in genus (i.e., conjunctive or disjunctive) and species (i.e., implication, opposition, or coincidence). So the contradictory of a conjunctive conditional is conjunctive, of a disjunctive is disjunctive; and within each, the contradictory of an implicative is implicative, of an oppositive is oppositive, and of a coincidental is coincidental. He emphasizes that this applies to the explicit contradictory; otherwise, a universal compound proposition has as its contradictory a particular exclusive (mani'a al-khuluww) which is a conditional. This is a nuanced point showing that contradiction is not always straightforward; it depends on the logical form.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the contradictory of a conditional proposition?
A) It must be a different genus (conjunctive vs disjunctive).
B) It must have the same genus and species, differing only in quality and quantity.
C) It can be any proposition that is false when the original is true.
D) It must be a simple categorical proposition.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition preserves structure and species while inverting quality and quantity, a principle that ensures logical opposition is well-defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Every human is not necessarily an animal' (i.e., some humans are not animals necessarily?), find its contradictory using the rules for simple categorical modals.
?
Take the conditional 'If Zayd is awake, then he is aware.' Construct its contradictory and verify that it is also a conjunctive conditional differing in quality.

Conversion ('Aks Mustawi): Definition and Conditions
Author defines conversion and discusses conditions of preserving truth and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid conversions of categorical and conditional propositions and apply the conditions of truth and quality preservation to Islamic science arguments.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps extremes while preserving truth and quality—a strict formal condition.

In the previous lesson, we learned about immediate inferences. Now we dive into one of the most important: conversion ('aks mustawi). This lesson explains the operation of swapping the subject and predicate (or antecedent and consequent) and the two conditions that guarantee the logical validity of the converse: preservation of truth and preservation of quality. Pay close attention to the discussion about whether the quality condition is redundant, as it sharpens your understanding of formal versus material truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation
Conversion involves exchanging the two extremes of a proposition. For categoricals, the subject becomes the predicate and vice versa. For conditionals, the antecedent becomes the consequent and vice versa.
This is the core mechanical step.
2
Apply the truth condition
The converse must be true whenever the original is true. This is a conditional requirement: if the original holds, the converse must hold. It does not require both to be actually true in reality.
This condition ensures the inference is truth-preserving.
3
Apply the quality condition
The converse must have the same quality (affirmative or negative) as the original. The author argues this condition is already implied by the truth condition, but it is explicitly mentioned to avoid confusion.
Violating quality usually leads to falsehood in form, though material exceptions exist.
4
Analyze the redundancy argument
The commentator claims that the quality condition is redundant because if quality changes, truth cannot be preserved in general. He uses the example: 'Every human is a speaker' (A) does not convert to 'Some speaker is not human' (O) even if that happens to be true materially.
This tests your ability to distinguish formal from material truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَقِيمُ وَالْمُسْتَوِي تَبْدِيلُ طَرَفَيْ الْقَضِيَّةِ)، بأن يجعل المحمول عنوان الموضوع، وعنوانه محمولا في الحملية، ونفس التالي مقدما والمقدم تاليا في الشرطية، (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ)، أي كون الحاصل بعد التبديل بحيث لو فرض صدق الأصل لزم صدقه، لا وجوب صدقهما في الواقع، (وَالْكَيْفِ(۲))،

Chapter on Conversion. Straight and even conversion is the exchange of the two extremes of the proposition, by making the predicate the title of the subject and its title the predicate in categoricals, and the consequent itself the antecedent and the antecedent the consequent in conditionals, with preservation of truth, i.e., that what results after the exchange is such that if the original is assumed true, its truth follows, not that both are necessarily true in reality, and preservation of quality. (1) Refer in straight conversion to al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat with Sharh al-Tusi: 1/321-340, al-Mi'yar by Imam al-Ghazali: 83-85, Mantiq al-Talwihat by al-Suhrawardi: 41-44, al-Shamsiyya with Sharh al-Qutb and Glosses of al-Sayyid and 'Abd al-Hakim: 2/140-169, al-Matali' with Sharh al-Qutb: 124-135, Sharh al-Khabisi on al-Tahdhib with Hashiya al-'Attar: 329-352, al-Burhan by al-Kulanbawi: 28-30. (2) This condition is not merely terminological; rather, they found by induction that if the conversion were not like that, it would not be true with the original in most materials, contrary to some materials, as when the predicate is more general than the subject; then it would be true in the conversion of the affirmative negative partial, like 'Some animal is not human' true with our saying 'Every human or some human is animal', so it is mentioned. Do you not see that 'Some speaker is not human' is not true in the conversion of our saying 'Every human is a speaker' while it is true, and likewise 'Some human is not' is not true in the conversion of our saying 'Some animal is human'? And what appears of truth with difference in quality as in the mentioned example is not by virtue of essence, but due to the particular matter. And what is intended by the condition of truth is truth by virtue of essence, not other. Yes, if he said 'with preservation of quality='
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of conversion ('aks mustawi) in logic. The author, commenting on a base text, defines conversion as swapping the two extremes (subject and predicate in categoricals, antecedent and consequent in conditionals) while preserving truth and quality. The preservation of truth means that if the original proposition is true, the converted one must also be true; this is a conditional requirement, not that both are actually true in the world. The preservation of quality means the converted proposition retains the same affirmative or negative character. The passage then includes a footnote that lists major references on conversion. Another footnote discusses the condition of preserving quality, arguing that it is redundant because the truth condition already implies it. The author explains that if quality is not preserved, truth cannot be preserved as a general rule; any apparent counterexample is due to the specific matter, not the logical form. For instance, converting 'Every human is a speaker' to 'Some speaker is not human' would be false in logical form, even if accidental examples might appear true. Thus, the intended condition of truth is formal, not material. This commentary helps clarify the precise conditions for valid conversion in syllogistic logic.

Definition of Al-'Aks Al-Mustawi and Its Conditions
Definition of conversion and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-'aks al-mustawi and state its two conditions of preserving truth and quality.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality.

In this lesson, we learn what a direct conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) is and what conditions it must satisfy. The author defines it as exchanging the two extremes of a proposition while preserving truth and quality. We will break down this definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation: exchange of extremes
The definition states that conversion is the exchange (tabdīl) of the two extremes (ṭarafay) of a proposition. For a categorical proposition, this means swapping subject and predicate. For a conditional, it means swapping antecedent and consequent.
2
Distinguish categorical and conditional cases
The author explicitly includes both types. In the categorical, the predicate becomes the subject's title and the subject becomes the predicate. In the conditional, the consequent becomes the antecedent and the antecedent becomes the consequent.
3
Understand the condition of preserving truth
'Preserving truth' (baqā' al-ṣidq) means that if the original proposition is assumed true, the converted proposition must also be true. It does not require that both are actually true in reality; only a conditional relationship is needed.
This avoids the mistake of thinking conversion demands factual truth.
4
Understand the condition of preserving quality
The quality (al-kayf) – affirmative or negative – must remain the same. If the original is affirmative, the converse is affirmative; if negative, the converse is negative.
Without this condition, truth might not be preserved in general materials.
5
Apply to concrete examples
Test the definition: from 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative), the valid converse is 'Some forbidden is intoxicant' (particular affirmative) – preserving truth and quality. But 'Every human is mortal' cannot convert to 'Every mortal is human' because truth would fail.
The condition of quality prevents invalid quantifier changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العكس المستقيم والمستوي تبديل طرفي القضية، بأن يجعل المحمول عنوان الموضوع، وعنوانه محمولا في الحملية، ونفس التالي مقدما والمقدم تاليا في الشرطية، مع بقاء الصدق، أي كون الحاصل بعد التبديل بحيث لو فرض صدق الأصل لزم صدقه، لا وجوب صدقهما في الواقع، والكيف، أي إن كان الأصل موجبا كان الحاصل موجبا، وإن كان سالبا كان سالبا.
The straightforward and direct conversion is the exchange of the two extremes of the proposition — by making the predicate the subject's title and its title the predicate in the categorical, and the consequent itself antecedent and the antecedent consequent in the conditional — while preserving truth, i.e., that what results after the exchange is such that if the original is assumed true, its truth follows, not that both are necessarily true in reality, and [preserving] quality, i.e., if the original is affirmative, the result is affirmative, and if negative, negative.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage defines the logical operation of conversion ('aks mustawi). The commentator expands on the definition by specifying the exchange in both categorical and conditional propositions. The condition 'preserving truth' means the converted proposition must follow from the original, not that they are both true in fact. Additionally, the quality (affirmation or negation) must be preserved. This ensures that the conversion is valid in all materials, not just accidentally. The commentator emphasizes that the condition of preserving quality is included because without it, truth may not be preserved in general, though in some materials it might hold — but the requirement is for the conversion itself, not particular cases.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
بقاء الصدق
الكيف

Key Takeaway

When Does a Proposition Qualify as the Converse (al-'Aks)?
Author clarifies when the term 'aks refers to the proposition resulting from conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition resulting from term exchange qualifies as the technical converse according to the definition of 'most specific necessary consequence'.

Core Idea
Only the most specific necessary consequence of term exchange is called the converse.

In the logic of immediate inference, the term 'aks (conversion) has a precise technical meaning. This lesson clarifies that not every proposition that follows from swapping terms qualifies as the converse. Only the narrowest necessary consequence earns that label. This precision prevents confusion when multiple implications arise.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the operation from the result
Recognize that 'aks can refer to the act of conversion or the resulting proposition. Here it's about the proposition.
2
Understand 'most specific necessary consequence'
The converse must be the logically strongest (most specific) proposition that necessarily follows from the original after term exchange.
The Arabic term is 'أخص لازم' (akhass lazim).
3
Apply to a concrete example
Given 'Every A is B', the possible consequences include 'Some B is A' (necessary) and 'Every B is A' (not always true). Only 'Some B is A' is the most specific necessary consequence.
4
Test a non-example
A proposition that is true with the original but is not the most specific (e.g., a broader statement) is not called a converse by convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يطلق العكس على القضية الحاصلة منه، إذا كانت أخص لازم، أي لو كان هناك بعد التبديل لوازم متعددة يقال العكس لأخصها، فالقضية الحاصلة بعد التبديل الصادقة معه من غير لزوم أو مع لزوم، وتكون أعم من أخرى، لا تسمى عكسا اصطلاحا.
And sometimes the term 'aks is applied to the proposition resulting from it, if it is the most specific necessary consequence, i.e., if after the exchange there are multiple consequences, the term 'aks is said for the most specific of them. So the proposition resulting from the exchange which is true with it without necessity or with necessity, and is more general than another, is not called 'aks by convention.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage clarifies a terminological nuance: the word 'aks can refer to the proposition that results from conversion, but only when that proposition is the most specific necessary consequence of the original. If there are multiple propositions that follow from the original after swapping terms, only the narrowest (most specific) one is technically called the converse. A broader proposition that also follows is not considered the converse by convention. This refinement prevents confusion when multiple consequences exist.

Key Terms
العكس
أخص لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are converses.
B) 'Some B is A' because it is the most specific necessary consequence.
C) 'Every B is A' because it is stronger.
D) Neither, because conversion requires preserving truth only.

Key Takeaway
The technical term 'aks (converse) is reserved for the most specific necessary consequence of term exchange, not for any broader proposition that follows.

The Rule: All Affirmatives Convert as Particulars
The commentator states and justifies the rule for simple conversion of all affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of simple conversion to affirmative categorical and conditional propositions and explain why universal conversion is invalid.

Core Idea
Every affirmative converts as a particular; the predicate's generality blocks universal conversion.

This lesson unpacks the foundational rule of simple conversion: every affirmative proposition, whether categorical or conditional, universal or particular, validly converts only as a particular. The passage provides the logical justification and explains why universal conversion is impossible, a key distinction for sound immediate inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule statement
Find the sentence that explicitly states the rule for all affirmatives. Note the terms 'مطلقًا' (absolutely) and 'تنعكس جزئية' (converts as particular).
The rule is: 'والموجبة مطلقا، كلية كانت أو جزئية، حملية كانت أو شرطية تنعكس جزئية'.
2
Understand the justification
The passage gives a reason: affirmation is a conjunction of subject and predicate in one essence (for categoricals) or connection of antecedent and consequent (for conditionals). This entails a reciprocal relation, yielding at least a particular converse.
The justification uses variables (ج and ب) to show that if some B is A, then some A is B.
3
Why universal conversion fails
The predicate may be more general than the subject. If every A is B, it does not follow that every B is A, because B could have individuals that are not A. The text says 'لجواز عموم المحمول' (due to possibility of the predicate being more general).
Example: Every human is animal does not convert to Every animal is human.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the universal affirmative 'Every khamr is haram'. Write its converse and check if it is universal or particular. Verify that the converse 'Some haram is khamr' is true but 'Every haram is khamr' is false.
This shows the practical application: conversion preserves truth only as a particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي. واعلم أن السالبة الجزئية من حيث الكمية والكيفية غير آبية عن الانعكاس، وإلا لم تنعكس الخاصتان منها فذكر عدم انعكاسها ههنا غير مناسب، كما ذكر انعكاس السالبة الكلية مع عدم انعكاسها في كثير من الموجهات. والله أعلم. (وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا، كلية كانت أو جزئية، حملية كانت أو شرطية) تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ لـ (ج) و(ب) بالاتحاد في ذات واحدة، وللمقدم والتالي بالاتصال على تقدير، فكما أن تلك (ب) فكذلك (ج) فبعض (ب) (ج)، وكما أن التالي لازم للمقدم على ذلك التقدير، كذلك المقدم لازم للتالي على ذلك التقدير، فبعض ما يتحقق التالي تحقق المقدم، وهو مفهوم العكس. (وَلَا) تنعكس كُلَّيَّةً، لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي، وامتناع ثبوت الأخص لكل أفراد الأعم، وكذا لزوم الأخص للأعم كليا. ولما كان لناقض أن ينقض هذا الحكم بقولنا: كل شيخ كان شابا، لكذب عكسه، وهو: بعض شاب كان شيخا، وبقولنا: بعض النوع إنسان، لكذب بعض الإنسان نوع الأول، أجاب بقوله: (وَقَوْلُنَا كُلُّ شَيْخِ كَانَ شَابَّا) الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهِ النِّسْبَةُ؛ إذ المحمول فيه كان شابا، لا شاب وحده، فَعَكْسُهُ بَعْضُ مَنْ كَانَ شَابًا شَيْخٌ، بجعل النسبة موضوعا. وفيه ورود ظاهر؛ فإن كان رابطة كما تقدم، وليس جزء من المحمول، بل المحمول «شاب» فقط، ففي العكس يصير موضوعا، و«الشيخ» الموضوع محمولا، والرابط على حاله، فاستقر النقض في مقره، ولم يتم الدفع. فالصواب في الجواب أن يقال: إن هذه القضية حكم فيها بثبوت المحمول ثبوتا مؤقتا بزمان الماضي، فهي مطلقة وقتية إن لم يعتبر فيها الضرورة، ووقتية مطلقة إن اعتبرت، وسيتضح لك أنهما تنعكسان مطلقة عامة، فحينئذ عكسها بعض شاب شيخ بالفعل، وهي صادقة. ومن ههنا ظهر فساد ما قيل: إن عكسها بعض شاب يكون شيخا؛ لأنها أيضا مطلقة وقتية، لكن الوقت فيها غير الوقت الذي كان في الأصل، والمطلقة الوقتية لا تنعكس مطلقة وقتية. والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب. وأجاب عن الثاني بقوله: (وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ) باعتبار الحمل المتعارف الذي نحن في بيان عكسه؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعٍ. وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ، وهو لا شيء من النوع بإنسان. (وَالسِّرُّ فِيهِ) أي كذب تلك القضية (أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ) على أفراد الموضوع، بأن يكون أفراده أفراد المحمول، أو على نفس الموضوع، بأن يكون هو نفسه فرد المحمول، وهو ههنا منتف؛ لأن أفراد الموضوع ليست أفراد الإنسان، كما لا يخفى، فكذبت تلك القضايا باعتبار الحمل المتعارف. (لَا) المعتبر (نَفْسُ مَفْهُومِهِ)، بأن يكون فردا للموضوع، يعني ليس المعتبر فيه كون نفس مفهوم المحمول فردا للموضوع؛ إذ مفاده عينية المحمول لفرد الموضوع، وهو شأن الحمل الأولى. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُنْفَصِلاتِ وَالِاتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْجَدْوَى. فيه إشارة إلى أن هذه القضايا وإن كان لها عكوس صادقة، ويصدق عليها تعريفه، لكن لمما لم يرجع إلى طائل؛ فإن المنافاة والتوافق يكونان من الطرفين، فعلمك بأن هذا
Chapter on simple conversion. Know that the particular negative, in terms of quantity and quality, is not averse to conversion; otherwise its two special cases would not convert, so mentioning its non-conversion here is inappropriate, just as the conversion of the universal negative is mentioned along with its non-conversion in many modal propositions. And God knows best. The affirmative, absolutely, whether universal or particular, categorical or conditional, converts as particular, because affirmation is the conjunction of (j) and (b) by union in one essence, and of the antecedent and consequent by connection under a condition; just as that (b) is (j), so similarly (j) is (b), so some (b) is (j), and just as the consequent is necessary to the antecedent under that condition, so also the antecedent is necessary to the consequent under that condition, so some of what realizes the consequent realizes the antecedent, and that is the concept of conversion. And it does not convert as universal, due to the possibility of the predicate or consequent being more general, and the impossibility of the particular being affirmed of every individual of the more general, and likewise the impossibility of the particular being necessary for the more general universally. Since an objector could object to this ruling by our saying: Every old man was young, due to the falsity of its converse, which is: Some young man was old, and by our saying: Some species is man, due to the falsity of: Some man is type of the first, he answered by saying: Our saying: Every old man was young, the predicate in it is the relation; for the predicate in it is 'was young', not 'young' alone, so its converse is: Some who was young is old, by making the relation the subject. But there is an apparent flaw in this; for if it is a copula as previously, and not part of the predicate, rather the predicate is just 'young', then in the converse it becomes subject, and 'old' the subject becomes predicate, and the copula remains as it is, so the objection stands in its place, and the rebuttal is not successful. So the correct answer is to say: This proposition rules the establishment of the predicate as temporally established in past time, so it is a temporal absolute if necessity is not considered, and absolute temporal if considered, and it will become clear to you that they both convert as general absolute, then its converse is: Some young person is actually old, and it is true. From here the corruption becomes clear of what was said: that its converse is: Some young person will be old; because it is also a temporal absolute, but the time in it is other than the time in the original, and the temporal absolute does not convert as temporal absolute. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. And he answered the second by saying: Our saying: Some species is man is false, according to the customary predication whose converse we are explaining, due to the truth of: No man is a species. And it converts to its contradictory, which is: No species is man. And the secret in it, i.e., the falsity of that proposition, is that what is considered in customary predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate upon the individuals of the subject, such that its individuals are individuals of the predicate, or upon the subject itself, such that it itself is an individual of the predicate, and this is absent here; because the individuals of the subject are not individuals of man, as is not hidden, so those propositions are false according to customary predication. Not what is considered is the essence of its concept, i.e., that the concept itself is an individual of the subject, meaning what is considered in it is not that the very concept of the predicate is an individual of the subject; for its implication is the identity of the predicate with an individual of the subject, and that is the matter of primary predication. And there is no conversion for disjunctives and accidentals, due to the lack of benefit. In it there is an indication that these propositions, even if they have true converses and the definition applies to them, yet they do not lead to any advantage; for opposition and agreement are from both sides, so your knowledge that this
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rule for simple conversion of affirmative propositions. It begins by noting a parenthetical point: the particular negative is not averse to conversion, so mentioning its non-conversion in some contexts is inappropriate. The author then states the main rule: every affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, categorical or conditional, converts as a particular affirmative. The reason given is that affirmation involves the conjunction of subject (j) and predicate (b) in one essence or the connection of antecedent and consequent; thus the relation is reciprocal, yielding at least a particular converse. The author then explains why it cannot convert as universal: the predicate may be more general, so the converse cannot be universal. Two potential counterexamples are addressed. The first is 'Every old man was young.' The objector claims the converse 'Some young person was old' is false. The initial answer distinguishes the predicate as the relation 'was young' rather than just 'young', so the converse is 'Some who was young is old'. However, the commentator notes a flaw: if 'was' is a copula, not part of the predicate, the objection stands. The correct answer is that the proposition is temporal absolute (muṭlaqa waqtiyya) meaning it holds at a specific past time, and such propositions convert as general absolute, so the converse 'Some young person is actually old' is true. The second objection is 'Some species is man', which is false; its converse 'No man is a species' is true, contradicting the claim that the original converts to a particular affirmative. The answer explains that in customary predication (al-ḥaml al-muta'āraf), the predicate must apply to the individuals of the subject; here, the individuals of 'species' are not individual humans, so the proposition is false and its true converse is a universal negative. Finally, the passage states that disjunctive and accidental propositions have no useful conversion because the relation of opposition or agreement is already symmetric; there is no logical benefit.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الموجبة
الموجبة الجزئية
الموجبة الكلية

Checkpoint

A) A universal affirmative: 'Every haram is khamr'.
B) A particular affirmative: 'Some haram is khamr'.
C) A particular negative: 'Some haram is not khamr'.
D) It does not convert at all.

Key Takeaway
All affirmative propositions convert as particulars; universal conversion is invalid because the predicate is not necessarily equivalent in extension.

Handling Objections: Temporal Propositions and Customary Predication
The commentator responds to two objections against the conversion rule and clarifies the conditions for conversion, including temporal modality and customary predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve apparent counterexamples to the conversion rule by distinguishing temporal modality and customary predication.

Core Idea
Objections to conversion reveal the need to consider temporal scope and the type of predication.

The commentator addresses two objections that seem to violate the conversion rule. The first involves a temporal proposition ('Every old man was young'), the second involves a proposition with non-customary predication ('Some species is man'). Understanding the correct responses clarifies the conditions under which conversion is valid and why certain propositions are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Find the objection: 'Every old man was young' seems to have a false converse 'Some young person was old' because young people are not old in the present.
The objection challenges the rule that affirmatives convert as particulars.
2
Analyze the initial (flawed) response
The commentator first suggests that the predicate is the relation 'was young', so the converse is 'Some who was young is old', which is true. But this fails if 'was' is a copula.
The flaw is noted: the copula is not part of the predicate.
3
Understand the correct answer
The proposition is a temporal absolute (muṭlaqa waqtiyya): it asserts that the predicate held at some past time. Temporal absolutes convert as general absolutes, so the correct converse is 'Some young person is (actually) old', which is true.
This shows that temporal modality affects conversion: the time of the converse is not the same as the original, but it is still true.
4
Analyze the second objection
The objection: 'Some species is man' seems false, and its converse 'Some man is a species' is also false, but the rule would predict the converse is true. However, the commentator shows that the original is false under customary predication.
Customary predication requires that the predicate applies to the individuals of the subject; here, 'species' is not a type of human.
5
Understand the definition of customary predication
The passage defines customary predication: the truth of the predicate concept upon the individuals of the subject. In 'Some species is man', the individuals of 'species' are not individual humans, so it is false. Its true converse is 'No man is a species'.
Thus the objection dissolves: the falsity of the original is due to a different type of predication, not a failure of conversion.

فصل في العكس المستوي. واعلم أن السالبة الجزئية من حيث الكمية والكيفية غير آبية عن الانعكاس، وإلا لم تنعكس الخاصتان منها فذكر عدم انعكاسها ههنا غير مناسب، كما ذكر انعكاس السالبة الكلية مع عدم انعكاسها في كثير من الموجهات. والله أعلم. (وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا، كلية كانت أو جزئية، حملية كانت أو شرطية) تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ لـ (ج) و(ب) بالاتحاد في ذات واحدة، وللمقدم والتالي بالاتصال على تقدير، فكما أن تلك (ب) فكذلك (ج) فبعض (ب) (ج)، وكما أن التالي لازم للمقدم على ذلك التقدير، كذلك المقدم لازم للتالي على ذلك التقدير، فبعض ما يتحقق التالي تحقق المقدم، وهو مفهوم العكس. (وَلَا) تنعكس كُلَّيَّةً، لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي، وامتناع ثبوت الأخص لكل أفراد الأعم، وكذا لزوم الأخص للأعم كليا. ولما كان لناقض أن ينقض هذا الحكم بقولنا: كل شيخ كان شابا، لكذب عكسه، وهو: بعض شاب كان شيخا، وبقولنا: بعض النوع إنسان، لكذب بعض الإنسان نوع الأول، أجاب بقوله: (وَقَوْلُنَا كُلُّ شَيْخِ كَانَ شَابَّا) الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهِ النِّسْبَةُ؛ إذ المحمول فيه كان شابا، لا شاب وحده، فَعَكْسُهُ بَعْضُ مَنْ كَانَ شَابًا شَيْخٌ، بجعل النسبة موضوعا. وفيه ورود ظاهر؛ فإن كان رابطة كما تقدم، وليس جزء من المحمول، بل المحمول «شاب» فقط، ففي العكس يصير موضوعا، و«الشيخ» الموضوع محمولا، والرابط على حاله، فاستقر النقض في مقره، ولم يتم الدفع. فالصواب في الجواب أن يقال: إن هذه القضية حكم فيها بثبوت المحمول ثبوتا مؤقتا بزمان الماضي، فهي مطلقة وقتية إن لم يعتبر فيها الضرورة، ووقتية مطلقة إن اعتبرت، وسيتضح لك أنهما تنعكسان مطلقة عامة، فحينئذ عكسها بعض شاب شيخ بالفعل، وهي صادقة. ومن ههنا ظهر فساد ما قيل: إن عكسها بعض شاب يكون شيخا؛ لأنها أيضا مطلقة وقتية، لكن الوقت فيها غير الوقت الذي كان في الأصل، والمطلقة الوقتية لا تنعكس مطلقة وقتية. والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب. وأجاب عن الثاني بقوله: (وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ) باعتبار الحمل المتعارف الذي نحن في بيان عكسه؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعٍ. وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ، وهو لا شيء من النوع بإنسان. Conversion of Temporal Absolute Propositions: The Time Condition
Critique of an incorrect conversion of a temporal absolute proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the time condition required for a valid conversion of a temporal absolute proposition and critique a flawed conversion attempt.

Core Idea
A temporal absolute converts only if the time in the converse matches the time in the original.

The author critiques a common error regarding the conversion of the temporal absolute proposition 'some young is old'. He explains that the original proposition refers to a specific past time (e.g., someone who is now old was once young), so its correct converse must also refer to that same past time (i.e., 'some old was young'). The mistaken claim that the converse is simply 'some young is old' fails because it changes the time reference. This lesson will help you recognize the critical role of temporal identity in immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original proposition is 'some young is old' considered as a temporal absolute (mutlaqa waqtiyya) referring to a specific past time.
2
Determine the correct converse
According to the author, the correct converse is 'some old is young' (i.e., 'some old was young'), which is also a temporal absolute but with the same time.
3
Analyze the flawed converse
The flawed claim says the converse is 'some young is old' (i.e., some young person is old now), which would be a different temporal absolute with a different time.
4
Test temporal identity
Check whether the time in the converse matches the time in the original. If not, the conversion fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: 'with a past time, so it is an absolute temporal if necessity is not considered, and a temporal absolute if considered, and it will become clear to you that both convert as a general absolute; then its converse 'some young is old actually' is true. From here it becomes clear the corruption of what was said: that its converse 'some young is old' is also an absolute temporal, but the time in it is different from the time that was in the original, and the absolute temporal does not convert as an absolute temporal. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. And he answered the second by his saying: 'And our saying 'some species is human' is false' according to the customary predication which we are explaining its converse; due to the truth of 'nothing of the human is a species'. And it converts to its contradictory, which is 'nothing of the species is human'. And the secret in it, i.e., the falsity of that proposition, is that what is considered in customary predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate over the individuals of the subject, in that its individuals are individuals of the predicate, or over the subject itself, in that it is itself an individual of the predicate, and here it is absent because the individuals of the subject are not individuals of human, as is not hidden, so those propositions are false according to customary predication. (Not) what is considered (is the concept itself), that it is an individual of the subject, meaning what is considered is not that the concept itself of the predicate is an individual of the subject; for its import is the identity of the predicate with the individual of the subject, which is the matter of primary predication. And there is no conversion for disjunctives and accidentals, due to lack of benefit. In it is an indication that these propositions, even if they have true converses and the definition applies to them, they do not return to any benefit; for the contradiction and agreement are from both sides, so your knowing that this is contradictory to that is your knowing that that is contradictory to this, and similarly in agreement, they said there is no conversion for them, as in the Hashiya. And let it be known that this is correct for the specific accidental, but as for the general accidental, it has no conversion in reality; for it may be composed of a false antecedent and a true consequent, so if it were converted, the antecedent would be true and the consequent false, so it would be false; for it requires the truth of the consequent. And Allah Almighty knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Bahr al-Ulum on the Sullam al-Ulum, specifically discussing the rules of conversion ('aks). The author begins by critiquing a false claim about the conversion of the temporal absolute proposition 'some young is old'. He explains that the original proposition is a temporal absolute (mutlaqa waqtiyya) referring to a specific past time, and its correct converse is also a temporal absolute but with the same time (i.e., 'some old was young' actually). The mistaken claim that the converse is simply 'some young is old' fails because the time frame shifts, and temporal absolutes must have the same time to convert. Next, the author addresses the proposition 'some species is human', showing it is false under customary predication (haml muta'arif) because the predicate 'human' does not apply to the individuals of the subject 'species' (since species are not humans). The real converse is 'nothing of the human is a species', which is its contradictory. He then states that there is no useful conversion for disjunctive (munfasil) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) propositions. Although they may have true converses in some sense, they yield no benefit because the relation of contradiction or agreement is symmetric. Specifically, for the general accidental (which consists of a conditional with a false antecedent and true consequent), conversion would produce a false proposition because the antecedent would become true and the consequent false. Thus, such conversions are invalid. The author concludes by noting that the ruling applies only to specific accidentals, not general ones, and leaves the ultimate truth to Allah.

Key Terms
مطلقة وقتية
عكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Bahr al-Ulum, why is the conversion 'some young is old' (as a temporal absolute) invalid?
A) Because the original is a necessary proposition.
B) Because the converse uses a different time than the original.
C) Because the converse is a universal proposition.
D) Because conversion is only valid for negatives.

Key Takeaway
A temporal absolute proposition converts only if the time remains the same; otherwise the conversion is invalid.

Customary Predication (al-Haml al-Muta'arif) and Conversion
Analysis of customary predication and conversion of a false particular affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a proposition under customary predication and derive its correct converse.

Core Idea
Under customary predication, a proposition may be false even if it seems true under primary predication; conversion then yields its contradictory.

The author examines the proposition 'some species is human' within the context of customary predication (al-haml al-muta'arif). He shows that it is false because the individuals of 'species' are not individuals of 'human'. Its correct converse is 'nothing of the human is a species', which is the contradictory of the original. Understanding this nuance is essential for proper conversion in contexts where predication is not based on conceptual identity.

Guided Path
1
State the proposition under scrutiny
The proposition is 'some species is human' (بعض النوع إنسان).
2
Define customary predication
In customary predication, the predicate's concept must be true of the individuals of the subject, or the subject itself must be an individual of the predicate.
3
Check the truth under customary predication
The individuals of 'species' are specific species like horse, dog, etc. These are not individuals of 'human'. Therefore the proposition is false.
4
Find the correct converse
The true proposition that converts is 'nothing of the human is a species' (لا شيء من الإنسان بنوع), which is the contradictory of the original.
5
Explain the 'secret'
The secret is that what matters is the truth of the predicate's concept over the subject's individuals, not the identity of concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم بزمان الماضي، فهي مطلقة وقتية إن لم يعتبر فيها الضرورة، ووقتية مطلقة إن اعتبرت، وسيتضح لك أنهما تنعكسان مطلقة عامة، فحينئذ عكسها بعض شاب شيخ بالفعل، وهي صادقة . ومن ههنا ظهر فساد ما قيل : إن عكسها بعض شاب يكون شيخا ؛ لأنها أيضا مطلقة وقتية، لكن الوقت فيها غير الوقت الذي كان في الأصل، والمطلقة الوقتية لا تنعكس مطلقة وقتية والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب، وأجاب عن الثاني بقوله: (وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ) باعتبار الحمل المتعارف الذي نحن في بيان عكسه؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعِ ) . وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ) ، وهو لا شيء من النوع بإنسان». (وَالسِّرُّ فِيهِ)، أي كذب تلك القضية (أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْحَمْلَ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَنْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ) على أفراد الموضوع، بأن يكون أفراده أفراد المحمول، أو على نفس الموضوع، بأن يكون هو نفسه فرد المحمول، وهو ههنا منتف؛ لأن أفراد الموضوع ليست أفراد الإنسان، كما لا يخفى، فكذبت تلك القضايا باعتبار الحمل المتعارف . (لَا) المعتبر (نَفْسُ مَفْهُومِهِ)، بأن يكون فردا للموضوع، يعني ليس المعتبر فيه كون نفس مفهوم المحمول فردا للموضوع ؛ إذ مفاده عينية المحمول لفرد الموضوع ، وهو شأن الحمل الأولى. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُنْفَصِلاتِ وَالاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْجَدْوَى). فيه إشارة إلى أن هذه القضايا. وإن كان لها عكوس صادقة، ويصدق عليها تعريفه، لكن لمما لم يرجع إلى طائل ؛ فإن المنافاة والتوافق يكونان من الطرفين، فعلمك بأن هذا مناف لذلك كان علمك بأن ذلك مناف لهذا ، وكذا في التوافق قالوا لا عكس لها، كذا في «الحاشية». وليعلم أن هذا صحيح في الاتفاقية الخاصة، وأما الاتفاقية العامة فلا عكس لها حقيقة ؛ فإنها ربما تلتئم من مقدم كاذب وتال صادق ، فلو انعكست كان المقدم صادقا والتالي كاذبا فيكذب؛ إذ لا بد فيها من صدق التالي. والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب.

The Inconvertibility of Disjunctive and Accidental Propositions
Statement that disjunctives and accidentals lack useful conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why disjunctive and accidental propositions cannot be converted beneficially and identify the special exception for general accidentals.

Core Idea
Disjunctives and accidentals are symmetric: converting them yields no new knowledge, so they have no useful conversion.

The author declares that disjunctive (munfasil) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) propositions have no conversion due to lack of benefit. Even if they could be converted formally, the resulting proposition would not provide new information because the relation (contradiction or agreement) is symmetric. However, he notes a special case: general accidentals, which consist of a false antecedent and true consequent, cannot be converted at all because conversion would make the antecedent true and consequent false. This lesson helps you recognize which propositions are logically inert under conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
Disjunctive propositions (e.g., 'either A or B') and accidental conditionals (where truth is coincidental).
2
Understand the symmetry
For disjunctives, if A is contradictory to B, then B is also contradictory to A. For accidentals, if the conditional is true by coincidence, the same relation holds from the opposite side.
3
Consider the benefit
Conversion would simply restate the same fact from the other side, providing no new information.
4
Special case: general accidentals
A general accidental has a false antecedent and true consequent. If converted, the antecedent becomes the consequent and vice versa, making the antecedent true and consequent false, which breaks the definition.
5
Conclude
Thus, disjunctives and specific accidentals have no useful conversion; general accidentals have no conversion at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Modal Conversion of Universal Negatives by Reductio
Author proves conversion of modal universal negatives via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the reductio proof for the conversion of a necessary universal negative and identify the role of the possible in the proof.

Core Idea
Modal universal negatives convert to themselves by reductio, using the possible to generate a contradiction.

This passage explains how modal universal negatives (necessary, permanent, general conditional, and general customary) convert to themselves via reductio ad absurdum. The author provides separate proofs for the permanent/general customary and for the necessary, and then notes an objection regarding the possible. Understanding this conversion is essential for manipulating modal propositions in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modal categories
The passage mentions four types: da'ima (permanent), daruriyya (necessary), mashruta 'amma (general conditional), and 'urfiyya 'amma (general customary). Note that the 'two permanent ones' include da'ima and daruriyya, and the 'two general ones' include the conditional and customary.
These are all universal negatives; the proof works for each pair.
2
Understand the reductio structure
Reductio (al-khalf) assumes the converse of the proposition is false, derives a contradiction, and thus concludes the converse is true. Here, the converse is the same proposition with swapped subject and predicate.
For example, 'nothing of (B) is (C)' converts to 'nothing of (C) is (B)'.
3
Apply reductio to permanent/general customary
Assume the converse is false; then its contradictory, 'some (B) is (C) actually or when it is (B)', is true. Combining this with the original proposition leads to 'some (B) is not (B)', a contradiction. Therefore the converse must be true.
The contradiction is immediate because the same term (B) both is and is not (B).
4
Apply reductio to the necessary
Assume the converse is false; then 'some (B) is (C) possibly' is true. Since the possible can be actualized, assume it is actual. This together with the original necessary negative yields 'some (B) is not (B) necessarily', a contradiction. Hence the converse is true.
The step from possible to actual is crucial; the author notes that the possible does not entail a contradiction by itself.
5
Examine the objection
The objection states that the possible actuality of 'some (B) is (C)' might arise from a different source, not from the assumed converse. For example, 'nothing of Zayd's ride is a donkey necessarily' – the possible 'some donkey is Zayd's ride possibly' could be true even if the converse 'no donkey is Zayd's ride necessarily' is false, because the actuality might be due to another cause.
The passage presents this as an apparent objection without resolving it, leaving it for further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْكُلَّيَّةِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ)، أي الدائمة والضرورية، (وَالْعَامَّتَانِ)، أي المشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة (كنفسها بِالْخُلْفِ)، أما في الدائمة والعرفية العامة فلو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائما أو ما دام (ب)) مع (لا شيء من (ج) (ب) دائما أو ما دام (ج)» لصدق نقيضه، وهو بعض (ب) (ج) بالفعل أو حين هو (ب)»، فينتجان بعض (ب) ليس (ب) دائما أو حين هو (ب)». وأما في الضرورية فلو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) بالضرورة» ، مع (لا شيء من (ج) (ب) بالضرورة يصدق بعض (ب) (ج) بالإمكان» ، وأمكن بعض (ب) (ج) بالفعل، فلو فرض ينتج مع الأصل «بعض (ب) ليس (ب) بالضرورة، والممكن لا يلزم من وقوعه محال ، فالنقيض محال ، فالعكس حق. ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أنه لا يلزم من صدق بعض (ب) (ج) الأصل إمكان فعليته معه ؛ لجواز أن يكون فعليته لغير الأصل، كما في «لا شيء من مركوب زيد بحمار بالضرورة، ونقيض عكسه (بعض الحمار مركوب زيد ،بالإمكان ولو فرض بالفعل لكان الحمار أيضا من أفراده، ويصدق عليه حمار ، لا سلبه.
As for modality, from the universal negatives, the two permanent ones convert, i.e., the necessary and the permanent, and the two general ones, i.e., the general conditional and the general customary, like themselves by reductio. As for the permanent and the general customary: if 'nothing of (B) is (C) permanently or as long as it is (B)' is not true together with 'nothing of (C) is (B) permanently or as long as it is (C)', then its contradictory would be true, i.e., 'some (B) is (C) actually or when it is (B)', so they produce 'some (B) is not (B) permanently or when it is (B)'. As for the necessary: if 'nothing of (B) is (C) necessarily' is not true together with 'nothing of (C) is (B) necessarily', then 'some (B) is (C) possibly' would be true, and 'some (B) is (C) actually' is possible, so if it is assumed, it produces with the original 'some (B) is not (B) necessarily', and the possible does not entail a contradiction from its occurrence, so the contradictory is impossible, so the converse is true. And there is an apparent objection to it: that from the truth of the original 'some (B) is (C)', it does not entail the possibility of its actuality with it; because it is possible that its actuality is due to something other than the original, as in 'nothing of Zayd's ride is a donkey necessarily', and the contradictory of its converse 'some donkey is Zayd's ride possibly', and if it is assumed actually, then the donkey would also be among its individuals, and 'donkey' would be true of it, not its negation.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of modal universal negative propositions using reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). The author divides these propositions into two pairs: the permanent ones (da'ima and daruriyya) and the general ones (mashruta 'amma and 'urfiyya 'amma). For the permanent and general customary negatives, the proof assumes the converse is false, which yields a contradictory true proposition (e.g., 'some (B) is (C) actually'), and from that together with the original false converse, a contradiction arises (e.g., 'some (B) is not (B)'), which is impossible. Therefore the original converse must be true. For the necessary negative, the assumption of false converse leads to a possible affirmative ('some (B) is (C) possibly'), and then assuming its actuality produces a contradiction with the original necessary negative. Since the possible does not necessarily lead to an impossibility, the reductio is valid. However, the author notes an objection: the possible actuality may not result from the assumed converse but from some other factor. The example given: 'Nothing of Zayd's ride is a donkey necessarily' – its converse should be 'No donkey is Zayd's ride necessarily' by reductio, but the objection argues that the possible actuality 'some donkey is Zayd's ride possibly' could be true even if the converse is false, because the actuality might come from a different scenario. This objection is presented but not refuted here; the author simply states it as an apparent objection.

ضرورية
الخلف
مشروطة عامة
عرفية عامة

According to the passage, how is the conversion of a necessary universal negative proposition (e.g., 'nothing of (B) is (C) necessarily') proved?
A) By direct conversion without any additional assumption.
B) By assuming the converse is false, deriving a possible affirmative, and then showing that assuming its actuality leads to a contradiction.
C) By using the fact that necessary propositions are always true.
D) By converting to a particular affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Modal universal negatives convert to themselves via reductio, with the necessary case requiring an extra step involving the possible.

Immediate Inference
