Why the Special Conditional Does Not Convert Identically
Refutation of the claim that the special conditional converts identically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a special conditional proposition does not convert identically and identify its correct conversion form.

Core Idea
Special conditionals convert as special accidental, not identically, because their descriptions conflict.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement of conversion rules for special conditional propositions. The author claimed that certain conditionals convert identically, but the commentator demonstrates why this is false and what the correct conversion form is. You will learn the logical reason based on mutual exclusivity of descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refined
Find the author's statement that the two special conditionals convert like themselves. The commentator says this is an oversight.
2
Understand the special conditional structure
A special conditional (مشروطة خاصة) has a condition on the predicate, e.g., 'Every B is C as long as it is B'. It is not a simple universal.
3
Analyze the mutual exclusivity argument
The two descriptions (condition of subject and predicate) are mutually exclusive in one essence by the first part of the original, yet they combine in the same essence by the second part. This prevents identical conversion.
4
Determine the correct conversion
The special conditional converts as a special accidental (عرفية خاصة), not identically. For example, 'Some B is not C as long as it is B' converts to 'Some C is not B as long as it is C' in a non-permanent sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِالْإِمْكَانِ ، ومتى لم ينعكس الأخص لم ينعكس الأعم؛ لتحققه في التي كذب فيها العكس. المادة (وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَّا الْخَاصَّتَانِ؛ فَإِنَّهُمَا تَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا ، هذا سهو، والصحيح أنهما تنعكسان عرفية خاصة ؛ فإن المشروطة الخاصة بشرط الوصف لا تنعكس كنفسها لما عرفت ؛ لِأَنَّ الْوَصْفَيْن)، أي وصفي الموضوع والمحمول (مُتَنَافِيَانِ فِي ذَاتِ وَاحِدَةٍ)، وليكن (د)، (بِحُكْمِ الْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ) من الأصل؛ إذ حكم فيه أن المحمول لا يكون ثابتا لذات الموضوع في أوقات ثبوته لها، (وَقَدْ اجْتَمَعَا فِيهَا بِحُكْمِ الْجُزْءِ الثَّانِي)، أي صدقا بالفعل عليها، وإن كان زمان صدق كل مغايرا لزمان الآخر بحكم اللادوام؛ إذ اللادوام موجبة ، فَتِلْكَ الذَّاتُ كَمَا لَمْ تَكُنْ (ب) مَا دَامَ (ج)) ، لا دائما، كذلك لَا تَكُونُ (ج) مَا دَامَ ((ب))، لا دائما، بحكم التنافي والتصادق، فبعض (ب) ليس (ج) ما دام (ب) لا دائما. وهذا الدليل غير دال على أن (ب) شرط لسلب (ج) ، فهذا لا يدل على انعكاس المشروطة كنفسها، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ) ، وقد عرفت أن مطلوب المصنف لم يتم. (وَمِنَ الْمُوجَبَاتِ تَنْعَكِسُ الْوُجُودِيَّتَانِ وَالْوَقْتِيَتَانِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ)، بل الوقتيتان المطلقتان والمطلقتان الوقتيتان أيضا مُطْلَقَةً عَامَّةٌ ، بِالْخُلْفِ)، تقريره: لو لم يصدق «بعض (ب) (ج) بالفعل» ، مع «كل (ج) أو بعضه (ب) بإحدى الجهات» لصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائما » ، وهو مع الأصل ينتج «بعض (ج) ليس (ج) دائما ، وهذا لا يتم على رأي الفارابي ؛ إذ كذب «بعض (ج) ليس (ج) ذائما لا يصح ؛ لأن أفراد (ج) يصدق عليه (ج) بالإمكان ، ويجوز أن لا يخرج هذا الصدق من القوة إلى الفعل فيصدق «بعض (ج) ليس (ج) دائما . نعم ، كذب بعض (ج) ليس (ج) بالضرورة» ضروري، فهذا لا يفيد إلا انعكاسها ممكنة.
By possibility, and when the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert; because it is realized in that in which the inverse is false. The material: 'And from the particular negative propositions, only the two special convert; for they convert like themselves.' This is an oversight, and the correct is that they convert as a special accidental; because the special conditional with the condition of the description does not convert like itself for what you know; because the two descriptions, i.e., the description of the subject and the predicate, are mutually exclusive in one essence, let it be (d), by the rule of the first part of the original; for it ruled that the predicate is not established for the essence of the subject in the times of its establishing for it, and they have combined in it by the rule of the second part, i.e., they are actually true of it, although the time of each is different from the time of the other by the rule of non-permanence; for non-permanence is affirmative. So that essence, just as it was not (b) as long as it is (c) — not always — likewise it is not (c) as long as it is (b) — not always — by the rule of mutual exclusion and co-truth. So some (b) is not (c) as long as it is (b) — not always. And this proof does not indicate that (b) is a condition for the negation of (c), so this does not indicate the conversion of the conditional like itself, and that is what is required. And you have known that the author's aim was not achieved. 'And from the affirmatives, convert the two existential, the two temporal, and the universal absolute.' But the two absolute temporal and the two temporal absolute also: universal absolute, by reduction to impossibility. Its explanation: if 'some (b) is actually (c)' were not true, while 'every (c) or some of it is (b) in one of the modes' is true, then 'no (b) is always (c)' would be true, and together with the original it yields 'some (c) is not always (c)'. And this does not hold according to al-Farabi's view; for the falsehood of 'some (c) is not always (c)' is not correct because the individuals of (c) are true of (c) by possibility, and it is possible that this truth does not emerge from potentiality to actuality, so 'some (c) is not always (c)' is true. Yes, the falsehood of 'some (c) is not necessarily (c)' is necessary, so this only benefits its conversion as possible.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the author's claim regarding the conversion of particular conditional propositions. The commentator first points out an error: the statement that the two special conditionals convert identically is wrong; they only convert as special accidental. The reason is that the descriptions of subject and predicate are mutually exclusive in one essence by the first part of the original proposition, yet they combine in that essence by the second part. Consequently, the essence is not (b) as long as it is (c) non-permanently, and similarly not (c) as long as it is (b) non-permanently, so some (b) is not (c) conditionally. But this does not establish that (b) is a condition for negating (c) universally. The commentator then discusses the conversion of affirmative propositions: existential, temporal, and absolute convert, and even the temporal absolute and absolute temporal convert as universal absolute by reductio ad absurdum. However, this reductio fails on al-Farabi's view because the falsehood of 'some (c) is not always (c)' is not necessary; only the falsehood of 'some (c) is not necessarily (c)' is necessary, so the conversion only yields possibility.

Al-Farabi's Objection to the Conversion of Affirmatives
Analysis of affirmative conversion and al-Farabi's critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze al-Farabi's objection to the reductio proof for converting affirmative propositions and explain its implications.

Core Idea
Al-Farabi's view on possibility disproves the reductio for universal absolute conversion of affirmatives.

This lesson continues the critical refinement, now focusing on the conversion of affirmative propositions. The commentator presents a reductio argument for converting certain affirmatives, but then notes that al-Farabi's position on possibility challenges its validity. You will examine the logical structure of the reductio and why it fails under al-Farabi's modal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion claim for affirmatives
The author states that existential, temporal, and absolute affirmatives convert. The commentator adds that even temporal absolute and absolute temporal convert as universal absolute.
2
Understand the reductio argument
The reductio: if the converse 'some (b) is actually (c)' were false, then 'no (b) is always (c)' would be true. This with the original 'every (c) is (b) in some mode' yields 'some (c) is not always (c)', a contradiction. So the converse must be true.
3
Recognize al-Farabi's objection
Al-Farabi holds that 'some (c) is not always (c)' can be true because the truth of (c) for its individuals is only possible, not necessarily actual. The contradiction only holds if the impossibility is necessary, which it is not.
4
Evaluate the outcome
The conversion only yields possibility, not necessity. The reductio fails to prove universal absolute conversion under al-Farabi's modal framework.

(وَمِنَ الْمُوجَبَاتِ تَنْعَكِسُ الْوُجُودِيَّتَانِ وَالْوَقْتِيَتَانِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ)، بل الوقتيتان المطلقتان والمطلقتان الوقتيتان أيضا مُطْلَقَةً عَامَّةٌ ، بِالْخُلْفِ)، تقريره: لو لم يصدق «بعض (ب) (ج) بالفعل» ، مع «كل (ج) أو بعضه (ب) بإحدى الجهات» لصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائما » ، وهو مع الأصل ينتج «بعض (ج) ليس (ج) دائما ، وهذا لا يتم على رأي الفارابي ؛ إذ كذب «بعض (ج) ليس (ج) ذائما لا يصح ؛ لأن أفراد (ج) يصدق عليه (ج) بالإمكان ، ويجوز أن لا يخرج هذا الصدق من القوة إلى الفعل فيصدق «بعض (ج) ليس (ج) دائما . نعم ، كذب بعض (ج) ليس (ج) بالضرورة» ضروري، فهذا لا يفيد إلا انعكاسها ممكنة.
By possibility, and when the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert; because it is realized in that in which the inverse is false. The material: 'And from the particular negative propositions, only the two special convert; for they convert like themselves.' This is an oversight, and the correct is that they convert as a special accidental; because the special conditional with the condition of the description does not convert like itself for what you know; because the two descriptions, i.e., the description of the subject and the predicate, are mutually exclusive in one essence, let it be (d), by the rule of the first part of the original; for it ruled that the predicate is not established for the essence of the subject in the times of its establishing for it, and they have combined in it by the rule of the second part, i.e., they are actually true of it, although the time of each is different from the time of the other by the rule of non-permanence; for non-permanence is affirmative. So that essence, just as it was not (b) as long as it is (c) — not always — likewise it is not (c) as long as it is (b) — not always — by the rule of mutual exclusion and co-truth. So some (b) is not (c) as long as it is (b) — not always. And this proof does not indicate that (b) is a condition for the negation of (c), so this does not indicate the conversion of the conditional like itself, and that is what is required. And you have known that the author's aim was not achieved. 'And from the affirmatives, convert the two existential, the two temporal, and the universal absolute.' But the two absolute temporal and the two temporal absolute also: universal absolute, by reduction to impossibility. Its explanation: if 'some (b) is actually (c)' were not true, while 'every (c) or some of it is (b) in one of the modes' is true, then 'no (b) is always (c)' would be true, and together with the original it yields 'some (c) is not always (c)'. And this does not hold according to al-Farabi's view; for the falsehood of 'some (c) is not always (c)' is not correct because the individuals of (c) are true of (c) by possibility, and it is possible that this truth does not emerge from potentiality to actuality, so 'some (c) is not always (c)' is true. Yes, the falsehood of 'some (c) is not necessarily (c)' is necessary, so this only benefits its conversion as possible.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the author's claim regarding the conversion of particular conditional propositions. The commentator first points out an error: the statement that the two special conditionals convert identically is wrong; they only convert as special accidental. The reason is that the descriptions of subject and predicate are mutually exclusive in one essence by the first part of the original proposition, yet they combine in that essence by the second part. Consequently, the essence is not (b) as long as it is (c) non-permanently, and similarly not (c) as long as it is (b) non-permanently, so some (b) is not (c) conditionally. But this does not establish that (b) is a condition for negating (c) universally. The commentator then discusses the conversion of affirmative propositions: existential, temporal, and absolute convert, and even the temporal absolute and absolute temporal convert as universal absolute by reductio ad absurdum. However, this reductio fails on al-Farabi's view because the falsehood of 'some (c) is not always (c)' is not necessary; only the falsehood of 'some (c) is not necessarily (c)' is necessary, so the conversion only yields possibility.

مشروطة خاصة
خلف

Al-Farabi's modal view shows that affirmative conversion via reductio only yields a possible converse, not a universal absolute.

The Supposition Method (iftiraad) in Simple Conversion
Explanation of the iftiraad method and its relation to the third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the iftiraad (supposition) method in simple conversion and reconstruct it as a third-figure categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Supposition in conversion is not a separate inference; it is a third-figure syllogism in disguise.

In this lesson, we explore a key technique in simple conversion: the method of supposition (iftiraad). This method involves assuming a single entity that possesses both the subject and predicate descriptions, thereby allowing a particular affirmative conclusion. The passage clarifies that this method is essentially a third-figure syllogism, and it addresses objections from Nasir al-Din al-Tusi and al-Farabi.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of iftiraad
Read the definition: we suppose a single entity (d) that has both the description of the subject (b) and the description of the predicate (c). Then (d) is (b) and (d) is (c).
The entity (d) is arbitrarily chosen as an instance of something that is both (b) and (c).
2
Follow the logical steps of supposition
From 'd is b' and 'd is c', we conclude 'some b is c'. This deduction mirrors a categorical syllogism.
The conclusion is particular affirmative because the premises involve a single instance.
3
Recognize the third-figure structure
The middle term (d) is the subject of both premises, which is the defining feature of the third figure (shakl thalith).
In standard third-figure form: All d are b, All d are c, therefore Some b are c. Here the premises are singular but analogous.
4
Examine the objections
al-Tusi objected that the terms are not distinct and thus no syllogism. The commentator refutes this by showing three distinct terms: the essence (d), description (c), and description (b). al-Farabi's view holds that d is only possibly c, so the conclusion is only possible.
The commentator rejects al-Tusi's claim and acknowledges al-Farabi's limitation.
5
Learn the inverse conversion introduction
The passage begins to describe another method: inverse conversion (al-'aks al-mun'akis) which uses reductio ad absurdum, but the argument is cut off.
This method will be completed in the next lesson.

Source Text

(وَالْعَكْس ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَنْعَكِسَ نَقِيضُ الْعَكْس لِيَرْتَدَّ إِلَى مَا يُنَافِي الْأَصْلَ)، فنقول: لو لم يصدق بعض (ب) (ج)» مع «كل (ج) (أ) وبعضه (ب)»
Chapter on Simple Conversion: 'And by supposition, which is that we suppose the essence of the subject something and the description of the subject and the description of the predicate are predicated of it. So we say: We suppose (c) which is (b) as (d), so (d) is (b) and (d) is (c); so some (b) is actually (c) from the third figure.' This is a text from the author that supposition is inference by the third figure. Then it is not permissible to explain the third figure by conversion. And the truth is that it is not a third figure; rather its outcome is that the descriptions of (c) and (b) combine in an essence, and if that essence is expressed by (b), then (c) is also established for it, so 'some (b) is (c)' is true. And this can also be used to prove the productivity of the third figure, as the master (al-Sheikh) verified. And what Nasir al-Din al-Tusi said—that it is not so because the terms are not distinct, nor is some of them predicated on others, so the form is not a syllogism, let alone from the third figure—this is something that provokes wonder from someone like him. For the terms are three definitely: the essence described by (c) called (d), the description (c), and the description (b); then the third figure can be formed. And God knows best. Then know that this also does not hold on al-Farabi's view; for (d) is not actually true of (c) on his view, but only by possibility, so only some (b) is (c) by possibility follows. 'And the inverse conversion, which is that the contradictory of the converse converts to return to what contradicts the original.' So we say: If 'some (b) is (c)' were not true, while 'every (c) is (a) and some of it is (b)'...
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on simple conversion, focusing on the method of supposition (iftiraad). The commentator begins by defining supposition: we assume a single entity (d) that has both the description of the subject (b) and the description (c) of the predicate. Then (d) is (b) and (d) is (c), so by the third figure we conclude 'some (b) is (c)'. The author of the matn (Sullam) used this to show that supposition is reasoning by the third figure. But the commentator argues that supposition is not a third figure syllogism; rather it directly shows that descriptions combine in one essence, so (c) is established for some (b). This method can also prove the third figure's productivity. The commentator then refutes Nasir al-Din al-Tusi's objection that the form is not a syllogism because terms are not distinct, stating that there are three distinct terms: the essence (d), description (c), and description (b), so the third figure can indeed be formed. Lastly, the commentator notes that even this supposition fails on al-Farabi's view because (d) is only possibly (c), so the conclusion is only possible, not actual. The passage then introduces another method called 'inverse conversion' (al-'aks al-mun'akis) which argues by showing that if the converse were false, it would contradict the original (reductio).

Proof by Contradiction for Conversion
Author explains proof by contradiction for conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction to establish the validity of a conversion for a categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Assuming the converse is false leads to a contradiction of the original.

The author introduces a method to prove that a proposition converts: assume the converse is false, derive the contradictory of the original, thereby confirming the converse. This proof underlies many conversion rules, but its validity depends on the modality involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its converse
Determine the subject and predicate in the original and swapped positions.
Example: original 'some B is J', converse 'some J is B'.
2
Assume the converse is false
This is the starting premise of the reductio ad absurdum argument.
The falsehood of the converse is taken as a temporary hypothesis.
3
Derive the contradictory of the original
Using logical steps, show that from the false converse we deduce a proposition that contradicts the truth of the original.
In the text, the additional premise 'every J is A and some J is B' is used.
4
Conclude the converse must be true
By reductio ad absurdum, since the assumption leads to a contradiction, the converse cannot be false; therefore it is true.
5
Verify with the given example
Apply the method to the example in the passage: 'some B is J' and 'every J is A and some J is B'.
The proof yields that 'no B is J always' contradicts the original, so the converse must hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْعَكْس ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَنْعَكِسَ نَقِيضُ الْعَكْس لِيَرْتَدَّ إِلَى مَا يُنَافِي الْأَصْلَ، فنقول: لو لم يصدق بعض (ب) (ج)» مع «كل (ج) (أ) وبعضه (ب)» لصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) دائما) ، وهو ينعكس (لا شيء من (ج) (ب) دائما ، وهو مناف للأصل. وهذا أيضا لا يتم على رأي الفارابي ؛ فإن الدائمة السالبة لا يمكن أن تنعكس كنفسها على رأيه ؛ لأنه قدرنا أن بكرا لم يركب إلا على الفرس، وزيد لم يركب على دابة أصلا، مع إمكان ركوبه على الفرس صدق قولنا لا شيء من مركوب بكر بالإمكان بمركوب زيد دائما»، وعكسه لا شيء من مركوب زيد بالإمكان بمركوب بكر دائما كاذب؛ لأن بعض مركوب زيد بالإمكان ، وهو الفرس، مركوب بكر بالفعل. نعم، يتم هذا الدليل لو ادعي عكسه ممكنة ؛ لأن نقيضه ،ضرورية، وهي تنعكس كنفسها على رأيه. هذا ، والعلم التام عند علام الغيوب. (وَ) تنعكس (الدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ)، بل الحينية المطلقة أيضا تنعكس حِينِيَّةً) مُطْلَقَةً ، بِالْوُجُوهِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ. تقرير الافتراض: إن الذات الموصوفة بـ(ج) بالفعل، و(ب) بالضرورة، أو دائما ما دام الذات أو في أوقات (ج)، وليكن (د) ، فـ(د) اجتمع فيها (ج) و (ب) في زمان واحد، فكما أنها (ب) في أحيان (ج) ، كذلك (ج) في أحيان (ب) ، فبعض (ب) (ج) حين هو (ب). وتقرير الآخرين واضح. وهذه الوجوه لا تتم على رأي الفارابي كما عرفت . ثم إنا لو قدرنا عدم ركوب زيد مدة عمره على دابة لصدق كل مركوب زيد بالإمكان حيوان بالضرورة، ولا يصدق بعض الحيوان مركوب زيد بالفعل حين هو حيوان. فالأشبه أن الموجبات كلها تنعكس ممكنة على رأي الفارابي.
And the conversion: it is that the contradictory of the converse reflects back to something that contradicts the original. So we say: if 'some B is J' were not true along with 'every J is A and some of it is B', then 'no B is J always' would be true, and it converts to 'no J is B always', which contradicts the original. And this also is not valid according to al-Farabi's view, because the universal negative with always cannot convert like itself in his view. For we suppose that Bakr only ever rode the horse, and Zayd never rode any animal at all, but with the possibility of him riding the horse, our statement 'nothing of Bakr's mount possibly is Zayd's mount always' is true, and its converse 'nothing of Zayd's mount possibly is Bakr's mount always' is false, because some of Zayd's mount possibly, namely the horse, is Bakr's mount actually. Yes, this proof is valid if the converse is claimed to be possible, because its contradictory is necessary, and it converts like itself in his view. This, and complete knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. And the two eternal (propositions) and the two universal (propositions) convert, rather the absolute temporal also converts as absolute temporal, by the mentioned methods. The exposition of the hypothesization: that the self described by J actually, and by B necessarily, or always as long as the self exists or at times of J, let it be D; so D combined J and B at one time. So just as it is B at times of J, similarly it is J at times of B, so some B is J when it is B. And the exposition of the others is clear. And these methods are not valid according to al-Farabi's view as you have known. Then, if we suppose that Zayd never rode any animal in his life, then 'every Zayd's mount possibly is an animal necessarily' is true, but 'some animal is Zayd's mount actually when it is an animal' is false. So the most plausible is that all affirmatives convert as possible according to al-Farabi's view.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction to establish the validity of conversion for certain categorical propositions, and then discusses how conversion applies to modal propositions with attention to al-Farabi's dissenting view. The author first describes a logical method: to prove that a proposition (e.g., some B is J) converts, we assume its converse is false, which implies the contradictory of the original, thereby contradicting the original assumed truth. The specific example given uses the conversion of the universal eternal negative. The author then notes that this proof does not hold for al-Farabi, because according to al-Farabi, a universal negative with 'always' cannot convert to an equivalent universal negative. The example of Bakr and Zayd illustrates this: the statement 'nothing of Bakr's mount possibly is Zayd's mount always' is true, but its converse 'nothing of Zayd's mount possibly is Bakr's mount always' is false, because the horse (which Zayd could ride) is an actual mount of Bakr. However, the proof works if the converse is claimed to be merely possible rather than necessary. The passage then moves to the conversion rules for 'da'imatan' (eternal affirmative and negative) and 'ammatan' (universal propositions) and also 'hiniyya' (temporal) propositions. It explains the iftiraad method for these: we hypothesize a self D that is actually J and necessarily B, or always B at times of J, and deduce that some B is J when it is B. These methods also fail for al-Farabi, and the author concludes that according to al-Farabi, all affirmative propositions convert as possible, not as actual. This serves as a nuanced discussion of modal conversion in the context of different logical schools.

Conversion of Modal Propositions and al-Farabi's Critique
Author discusses al-Farabi's objection to modal conversion rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Farabi's objection to the conversion of universal negative 'always' propositions and identify the correct modal conversion rules for different proposition types.

Core Idea
Modal conversion is not straightforward; al-Farabi shows that necessity and possibility affect conversion validity.

The author extends the discussion to modal propositions, reporting al-Farabi's view that a universal negative with 'always' does not convert to itself. The example of Bakr and Zayd's mounts illustrates this. The author then discusses conversion rules for eternal, universal, and temporal propositions, all of which fail under al-Farabi's analysis. Ultimately, all affirmative modal propositions convert as possibles according to al-Farabi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal proposition under consideration
Locate the proposition with its modal qualification, e.g., universal negative with 'always'.
Example: 'nothing of Bakr's mount possibly is Zayd's mount always'.
2
State al-Farabi's claim
Such a proposition does not convert to an equivalent universal negative with 'always'.
Al-Farabi asserts the converse is false when taken as the same modality.
3
Analyze the counterexample
Consider the Bakr and Zayd scenario: the original is true, but the converse with 'always' is false because the horse is both a possible mount for Zayd and an actual mount for Bakr.
4
Determine the correct converse
The correct converse is a possible negative, not a necessary universal negative.
The author notes the proof works if the converse is claimed to be possible.
5
Generalize to other modal types
Consider the standard rules for eternal, universal, and temporal propositions, and note that al-Farabi's view challenges their validity.
The author concludes that all affirmatives convert as possibles according to al-Farabi.

And the conversion: it is that the contradictory of the converse reflects back to something that contradicts the original. So we say: if 'some B is J' were not true along with 'every J is A and some of it is B', then 'no B is J always' would be true, and it converts to 'no J is B always', which contradicts the original. And this also is not valid according to al-Farabi's view, because the universal negative with always cannot convert like itself in his view. For we suppose that Bakr only ever rode the horse, and Zayd never rode any animal at all, but with the possibility of him riding the horse, our statement 'nothing of Bakr's mount possibly is Zayd's mount always' is true, and its converse 'nothing of Zayd's mount possibly is Bakr's mount always' is false, because some of Zayd's mount possibly, namely the horse, is Bakr's mount actually. Yes, this proof is valid if the converse is claimed to be possible, because its contradictory is necessary, and it converts like itself in his view. Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction to establish the validity of conversion for certain categorical propositions, and then discusses how conversion applies to modal propositions with attention to al-Farabi's dissenting view. The author first describes a logical method: to prove that a proposition (e.g., some B is J) converts, we assume its converse is false, which implies the contradictory of the original, thereby contradicting the original assumed truth. The specific example given uses the conversion of the universal eternal negative. The author then notes that this proof does not hold for al-Farabi, because according to al-Farabi, a universal negative with 'always' cannot convert to an equivalent universal negative. The example of Bakr and Zayd illustrates this: the statement 'nothing of Bakr's mount possibly is Zayd's mount always' is true, but its converse 'nothing of Zayd's mount possibly is Bakr's mount always' is false, because the horse (which Zayd could ride) is an actual mount of Bakr. However, the proof works if the converse is claimed to be merely possible rather than necessary. The passage then moves to the conversion rules for 'da'imatan' (eternal affirmative and negative) and 'ammatan' (universal propositions) and also 'hiniyya' (temporal) propositions. It explains the iftiraad method for these: we hypothesize a self D that is actually J and necessarily B, or always B at times of J, and deduce that some B is J when it is B. These methods also fail for al-Farabi, and the author concludes that according to al-Farabi, all affirmative propositions convert as possible, not as actual. This serves as a nuanced discussion of modal conversion in the context of different logical schools.

Conversion of Modal Categorical Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for perpetual, universal, and particular propositions with Farabi's counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct conversion for perpetual, universal, and particular modal propositions, and explain al-Farabi's counterexample to perpetual conversion.

Core Idea
Conversion rules for modal propositions depend on modality and temporal scope; al-Farabi showed that perpetual affirmatives convert only as possibles.

Conversion (عكس) is a fundamental operation in categorical logic, but its application to modal propositions requires careful attention to modality and temporality. In this passage, the author presents the standard rules for converting perpetual, universal, and particular propositions, then introduces al-Farabi's critical counterexample that shows perpetual affirmative conversion may fail. Understanding these nuances is essential for accurate syllogistic reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
Locate the four types mentioned: the two perpetuals (دائمتان: affirmative and negative), the two universals (عامتان: likely non-modal universals), and the two specifics (خاصتان: particular propositions). Note that the absolute temporal (حينية مطلقة) is also included.
2
Analyze the standard conversion formulation
Follow the formulation using variables (ج, ب, د). The author assumes an essence described by ج actually and by ب perpetually. The reasoning shows that if something is ب in times of ج, then it is ج in times of ب, yielding the converse. Understand the logical structure: it relies on the coexistence of the two predicates at the same time.
This is the standard Avicennan approach.
3
Examine al-Farabi's counterexample
Read the example: if Zayd never rode any animal, the proposition 'Everything Zayd possibly rides is necessarily an animal' is true perpetually, but its converse 'Some animal is actually ridden by Zayd when it is an animal' is false. Understand why this shows that perpetual affirmative conversion does not hold universally.
The counterexample exploits vacuous truth: the original is true because nothing actually satisfies the subject, but the converse requires an actual instance.
4
Derive the rule for particular propositions
The passage states that the two specifics (خاصتان) convert as temporal (حينية), not perpetual. The reason is that the concomitant of the universal is also a concomitant of the specific, and the absolute temporal is necessarily concomitant. Additionally, if the converse were perpetual, it would force the original predicate to be perpetual, contradicting the assumption.
This argument works on Ibn Sina's view; al-Farabi has a separate verification.

And the two perpetuals and the two universals convert; nay, the absolute temporal also converts as absolute temporal, by the mentioned aspects. Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion (عكس) of modal categorical propositions, specifically the conversion of 'perpetual' (دائم) and 'universal' (عام) propositions, and then the conversion of 'specific' (خاص) propositions. The author first states that the two perpetual and two universal propositions convert, and even the absolute temporal converts as absolute temporal, by the previously mentioned aspects. He then gives a formulation: assume an essence described by (ج) actually, and (ب) necessarily or perpetually as long as the essence or at times of (ج). Let that essence be (د). Then (د) has both (ج) and (ب) at one time. Thus, just as (د) is (ب) when it has (ج), it is also (ج) when it has (ب), so some (ب) is (ج) when it is (ب). This shows the conversion. However, these aspects do not hold according to al-Farabi's view. The author gives a counterexample: if Zayd never rode any animal in his life, then the perpetual statement 'everything Zayd possibly rides is necessarily an animal' is true, because whatever Zayd could possibly ride (like a horse) is an animal. But the converse 'some animal is actually ridden by Zayd when it is an animal' is false, because Zayd never rode anything. Therefore, according to al-Farabi, all affirmatives convert as possibles, not as perpetuals or universals. He then mentions a further verification regarding descriptive propositions but says it would be verbose. Next, he addresses the two specific (particular) propositions: they convert as temporal, not perpetual. The reason for the temporal conversion is that the necessary concomitant of the universal is a necessary concomitant of the specific, and the absolute temporal is necessary for the two universals. As for why the converse is not perpetual: if the converse were perpetual, then the subject's description would be perpetual, implying the predicate is perpetual (because in the original, the predicate is perpetual as long as the description holds). But the original assumed it was not perpetual, leading to a contradiction. This argument works on the view of the Shaykh (ibn Sina). On al-Farabi's view, there is another verification not detailed. The passage demonstrates the complexity of modal conversion and the disagreement between logicians.

The Two Definitions of Conversion by Contradiction
Author contrasts two definitions of 'aks al-naqid and declares the first as standard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the ancient and later definitions of 'aks al-naqid and identify which is standard in the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
'Aks al-naqid has two definitions: the older preserves quality, the newer flips it.

The author presents two competing definitions of conversion by contradiction and clarifies which one is used in the sciences. Understanding the difference is crucial for applying this immediate inference correctly in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the two definitions in the Arabic. The first definition (al-qudama') exchanges the contradictories of the two extremes while preserving both truth and quality. The second definition (al-muta'akhkhirun) makes the contradictory of the second term the first and the original first the second, with opposition of quality.
Notice the shift in quality requirement.
2
Compare the treatment of quality
In the first definition, quality (affirmative/negative) remains unchanged. In the second, the quality is reversed. This is a key difference that affects validity.
3
Determine which definition is authoritative in the sciences
The author states: 'والمعتبر في العلوم هو الأول' – the first definition is the one considered in the sciences. This tells you which one to use in Islamic disciplines like kalam and usul al-fiqh.
The second definition is only valid in conditionals, as explained later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عَكْسُ النَّقِيض : تَبْدِيلُ نَقِيضَي الطَّرَفَيْنِ، مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ)، بالمعنى الذي مر ، (وَالْكَيْفِ ، وَعِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ: جَعْلُ نَقِيض الثَّانِي أَوَّلًا وَعَيْنِ الْأَوَّلِ ثَانِيًا ، مَعَ مُخَالَفَةِ الْكَيْفِ وَمُحَافَظَةِ الصِّدْقِ ، وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْعُلُومِ هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ) ، بل يصح الثاني في الشرطيات؛ لجواز أن يكون نقيض التالي مما لا يستلزم عين المقدم، فلا يصدق سالبة ،لزومية، كما في مثالنا هذا: «كلما كان جوهر ذو وضع منقسما كان منقسما»، وعكسه على رأي المتأخرين: «ليس ألبتة كلما لم يكن جوهر ذو وضع منقسما كان منقسما، وقد برهن في الحكمة على أنه لو لم يكن جوهر ذو وضع منقسما كان منقسما، وعليه مدار إبطاله. وأما على رأي القدماء فعكسه قولنا: كلما لم يكن جوهر ذو وضع منقسما لم يكن منقسما» ، ، وعسى أن يكون صدقه ضروريا، وهو غير مناف لما برهن عليه في الحكمة ؛ فإن اللزومية السالبة التالي غير مناقض لموجبتها إذا كان المقدم محالا . هذا ، والعلم المطابق عند واهب العلوم. Chapter on Conversion by Contradiction. (Conversion by contradiction: exchanging the two contradictories of the two extremes, while preserving truth), in the sense that passed, (and the quality. And according to the later scholars: making the contradictory of the second the first and the same first as the second, with opposition of quality and preservation of truth, and the one considered in the sciences is the first). Actually careful: 'كان منقسما' is positive. So the later scholars' converse yields a negative universal conditional with a positive consequent? That seems problematic. Perhaps the OCR is garbled. Let's trust the text. The literal translation: 'Not at all whenever it is not that a substance possessing position is divisible, it is divisible.' That is indeed a negative universal conditional with a positive consequent. As for the ancients, its converse is our saying: 'Whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is not divisible.' And perhaps its truth is necessary, and it does not contradict what was demonstrated in wisdom; for the negative conditional of the consequent does not contradict its affirmative when the antecedent is impossible. This, and accurate knowledge is with the Bestower of sciences. Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage defines conversion by contradiction ('aks al-naqid), an immediate inference that inverts the two terms of a proposition while preserving truth. The author first gives the definition as 'exchanging the two contradictories of the two extremes' meaning the subject and predicate become their contradictories, but the truth value and quality (affirmative/negative) are preserved? Actually the text says 'with preservation of truth and the quality' but then the later scholars' definition changes the quality. The commentary clarifies: There are two definitions: the first (older) preserves both truth and quality; the second (later) inverts the terms by making the contradictory of the predicate the new subject and keeping the original subject as the new predicate, but with opposition of quality (i.e., if original was affirmative, the converse is negative and vice versa). The author states that the first definition is the one used in the sciences. Then he notes that the second definition only works for conditionals because in categoricals the contradictory of the predicate might not imply the original subject. He gives a metaphysical example: 'Whenever a substance possessing position is divisible, it is divisible.' This is a trivial conditional. The later scholars' converse yields a negative universal conditional: 'Not at all whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is divisible.' This seems to state that if it were not divisible, it would still be divisible? The author notes that in wisdom (Hikmah) it is proven that if a substance with position were not divisible, it would be divisible (due to its nature as substance with position). So the later converse appears to contradict this proven fact, but the author resolves by saying that the negative conditional of the consequent does not contradict its affirmative when the antecedent is impossible. The ancients' converse is a simple negative conditional: 'Whenever a substance with position is not divisible, it is not divisible' which preserves quality. Finally, the author gives a general rule: In conversion by contradiction, what happens to affirmatives is like what happens to negatives in simple conversion, and vice versa. This means the patterns of validity swap between the two conversion types.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
الشرطيات

Key Takeaway
The first definition of 'aks al-naqid (exchanging contradictories while preserving truth and quality) is the standard in Islamic sciences; the later definition with quality change is only valid for conditionals.

Testing the Later Definition with a Conditional Example
Author tests the later definition on a conditional example from metaphysics and explains its limited validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze why the later definition of 'aks al-naqid yields an invalid conclusion in categoricals but a potentially valid one in conditionals, using the metaphysical example provided.

Core Idea
The later definition fails in categoricals because the contradictory of the predicate may not imply the original subject.

The author provides a concrete conditional example from metaphysics to demonstrate that the later definition of 'aks al-naqid is only applicable to conditionals. He contrasts the result with the older definition and explains why the apparent contradiction with a proven fact is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the original conditional proposition
The original proposition is: 'كلما كان جوهر ذو وضع منقسما كان منقسما' (Whenever a substance possessing position is divisible, then it is divisible). This is a trivial conditional where the antecedent and consequent are identical.
This is a tautology, so it is necessarily true.
2
Apply the later definition to derive the converse
According to the later definition, the converse becomes: 'ليس ألبتة كلما لم يكن جوهر ذو وضع منقسما كان منقسما' (Not at all whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is divisible). This is a negative universal conditional with a positive consequent. The author notes that this seems to contradict a metaphysical proof that if such a substance were not divisible, it would be divisible.
The later definition flips the quality (affirmative to negative) and swaps the terms for their contradictories.
3
Apply the ancient definition to derive the converse
Using the ancient definition (preserving quality), the converse is: 'كلما لم يكن جوهر ذو وضع منقسما لم يكن منقسما' (Whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is not divisible). This is a simple contrapositive-like transformation and preserves truth.
The ancient definition yields a logically equivalent statement.
4
Understand why the later definition only works in conditionals
The author explains that the later definition is valid only in conditionals because in categoricals, the contradictory of the predicate might not necessitate the original subject. In this conditional example, the later converse appears false, but the author resolves it by noting that when the antecedent is impossible (which it is here, since the original antecedent is trivially true and its negation is impossible), the negative conditional does not contradict the affirmative conditional. Thus the later definition is not universally valid.
This shows the importance of using the correct definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وَحُكْمُ الْمُوجَبَاتِ هَهُنَا، أي في عكس النقيض (حُكْمُ السَّوَالِبِ فِي) العكس (الْمُسْتَقِيمِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ)، أي حكم السوالب ههنا حكم الموجبات
Chapter on Conversion by Contradiction. (Conversion by contradiction: exchanging the two contradictories of the two extremes, while preserving truth), in the sense that passed, (and the quality. And according to the later scholars: making the contradictory of the second the first and the same first as the second, with opposition of quality and preservation of truth, and the one considered in the sciences is the first). Rather the second is valid in conditionals because the contradictory of the consequent may not necessitate the same antecedent, so a universal negative conditional is not true, as in this example: 'Whenever a substance possessing position is divisible, then it is divisible.' Its converse according to the later scholars: 'Not at all whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is not divisible'? Actually the text says 'was munqasiman'? Need to check: The Arabic: «ليس ألبتة كلما لم يكن جوهر ذو وضع منقسما كان منقسما» which translates to 'Not at all whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is divisible'? Actually careful: 'كان منقسما' is positive. So the later scholars' converse yields a negative universal conditional with a positive consequent? That seems problematic. Perhaps the OCR is garbled. Let's trust the text. The literal translation: 'Not at all whenever it is not that a substance possessing position is divisible, it is divisible.' That is indeed a negative universal conditional with a positive consequent. And it has been demonstrated in wisdom that if it were not the case that a substance possessing position is divisible, it would be divisible, and upon it the refutation is based. As for the ancients, its converse is our saying: 'Whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is not divisible.' And perhaps its truth is necessary, and it does not contradict what was demonstrated in wisdom; for the negative conditional of the consequent does not contradict its affirmative when the antecedent is impossible. This, and accurate knowledge is with the Bestower of sciences. Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage defines conversion by contradiction ('aks al-naqid), an immediate inference that inverts the two terms of a proposition while preserving truth. The author first gives the definition as 'exchanging the two contradictories of the two extremes' meaning the subject and predicate become their contradictories, but the truth value and quality (affirmative/negative) are preserved? Actually the text says 'with preservation of truth and the quality' but then the later scholars' definition changes the quality. The commentary clarifies: There are two definitions: the first (older) preserves both truth and quality; the second (later) inverts the terms by making the contradictory of the predicate the new subject and keeping the original subject as the new predicate, but with opposition of quality (i.e., if original was affirmative, the converse is negative and vice versa). The author states that the first definition is the one used in the sciences. Then he notes that the second definition only works for conditionals because in categoricals the contradictory of the predicate might not imply the original subject. He gives a metaphysical example: 'Whenever a substance possessing position is divisible, it is divisible.' This is a trivial conditional. The later scholars' converse yields a negative universal conditional: 'Not at all whenever a substance possessing position is not divisible, it is divisible.' This seems to state that if it were not divisible, it would still be divisible? The author notes that in wisdom (Hikmah) it is proven that if a substance with position were not divisible, it would be divisible (due to its nature as substance with position). So the later converse appears to contradict this proven fact, but the author resolves by saying that the negative conditional of the consequent does not contradict its affirmative when the antecedent is impossible. The ancients' converse is a simple negative conditional: 'Whenever a substance with position is not divisible, it is not divisible' which preserves quality. Finally, the author gives a general rule: In conversion by contradiction, what happens to affirmatives is like what happens to negatives in simple conversion, and vice versa. This means the patterns of validity swap between the two conversion types.

Contraposition (ʿAks al-Naqīḍ) and Its Symmetry with Obversion
Definition of contraposition and its symmetric rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define contraposition and apply the symmetry rule between contraposition and obversion to determine the correct form of an immediate inference.

Core Idea
Contraposition is obversion's mirror: affirmatives behave like negatives.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) as presented by the author. He provides two definitions—one from the early logicians and one from the later—and clarifies which is used in the sciences. He then reveals a powerful symmetry: the rule for affirmatives in contraposition is identical to the rule for negatives in obversion, and vice versa. Understanding this symmetry will help you quickly apply the correct transformation without confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of contraposition
The author gives two definitions: (1) exchanging the negations of the two extremes while preserving truth and quality; (2) making the negation of the second the first and the same of the first the second, while changing the quality and preserving truth. Note which terms are exchanged and whether quality changes.
The first definition is attributed to the early logicians (al-mutaqaddimūn), the second to the later (al-muta'akhkhirūn).
2
Compare the two definitions
In the first definition, both truth and quality are preserved. In the second, quality must be changed. This is the key difference. The author states that the first definition is the one adopted in the sciences.
3
Apply the symmetry rule
The author says: 'the ruling of affirmatives here is the ruling of negatives in obversion, and vice versa.' This means that whatever transformation obversion applies to negatives, contraposition applies to affirmatives. For example, if obversion turns a universal negative into a universal affirmative, then contraposition turns a universal affirmative into a universal negative.
This rule is a mnemonic to help remember the correct form of contraposition without memorising separate cases.
4
Practice with an example
Take a proposition: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative). According to the first definition, contraposition requires exchanging the negations: 'Every non-forbidden is non-intoxicant'. Notice the quality stays affirmative. Now check the symmetry: obversion of 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' would give 'No intoxicant is non-forbidden' (universal negative). According to the rule, this is exactly what contraposition of the affirmative does, just with the terms negated.
This demonstrates the mirror relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَكْسُ النَّقِيض: تَبْدِيلُ نَقِيضَي الطَّرَفَيْنِ، مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَيْفِ، وَعِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ: جَعْلُ نَقِيض الثَّانِي أَوَّلًا وَعَيْنِ الْأَوَّلِ ثَانِيًا، مَعَ مُخَالَفَةِ الْكَيْفِ وَمُحَافَظَةِ الصِّدْقِ، وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْعُلُومِ هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ. وَحُكْمُ الْمُوجَبَاتِ هَهُنَا، أي في عكس النقيض، حُكْمُ السَّوَالِبِ فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَقِيمِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ، أي حكم السوالب ههنا حكم الموجبات في العكس المستقيم.
Contraposition: exchanging the two negations of the two extremes, while preserving truth and quality. According to the later scholars: making the negation of the second the first and the same of the first the second, while changing the quality and preserving truth, and what is considered in the sciences is the first. And the ruling of affirmatives here, i.e., in contraposition, is the ruling of negatives in obversion (the direct conversion), and vice versa, i.e., the ruling of negatives here is the ruling of affirmatives in obversion.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage defines contraposition ('aks al-naqid) and then states a symmetric rule between contraposition and obversion. The author first gives the classical definition as used by the early logicians (al-mutaqaddimun): exchanging the negations of the two extremes while keeping truth and quality. Then he mentions the definition of the later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun), which changes the positions and alters the quality, but he clarifies that the first definition is the one adopted in the sciences. Following that, he presents a rule: in contraposition, the behavior of affirmative propositions is like that of negative propositions in obversion (al-'aks al-mustaqim), and conversely, negatives in contraposition behave like affirmatives in obversion. This rule is a concise summary that helps students apply the inference correctly without confusion. For example, just as in obversion a universal affirmative ('Every A is B') becomes a universal negative ('No A is non-B'), in contraposition the analogous transformation involves negating both terms. The rule here is meta-linguistic: it tells us how to map the rules between the two conversions.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
العكس المستقيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) There is no relationship.

Key Takeaway
Contraposition exchanges the negations of subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality, and its rules mirror those of obversion.

Two Meanings of Negation in Contrapositive
Distinction between two meanings of naqīḍ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of 'negation' (naqīḍ) in contrapositive and apply the correct one to actual propositions.

Core Idea
Contrapositive turns on which negation you use: simple or modally contradictory.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial distinction: the term 'negation' (naqīḍ) has two meanings. In the chapter of conversion, it simply means differing by negation and affirmation without considering modality. In the chapter of relatives, it means modal contradiction—two propositions that cannot be true together of the same subject. This distinction resolves a key doubt about the contrapositive of actual propositions. Let's walk through it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings
Find where the author says 'by the two negations here is meant the two concepts differing by negation and affirmation...' and 'by them there – i.e., in the chapter of relatives – is meant the two concepts differing and contradictory in modality'. These are the two meanings. Note that the first ignores modal incompatibility, the second includes it.
2
Apply to actual propositions
For actual (fiʿliyya) propositions, the predicate is either equal to the subject or more general. If we use the second meaning (modal contradiction), the negation of the predicate becomes 'Not B always or by temporal possibility'. The author says the subject of contrapositive is not taken that way; al-Fārābī considered essential possibility.
3
Check the doubt's resolution
The doubt: if predicate is equal or broader, then its negation is narrower or equal, so contrapositive should yield a true universal. The solution: the equality claim holds only under the first meaning. Under the second, the negation of subject and predicate are not comparable in that way.
The author says 'if taken by the first consideration, we do not grant equality'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في عكس النقيض. ثمة، والبيان ههنا كالبيان ثمة. والتفصيل: أن الموجبات الكلية السبع التي لا ينعكس سوالبها بالاستقامة لا تنعكس بهذا العكس؛ لصدق «كل قمر فهو لا منخسف بالتوقيت»، وكذب «كل منخسف لا قمر». وههنا شك، هو: أن الموجبات الفعليات محمولاتها إما مساوية لموضوعاتها أو أعم منها، ونقيضا المتساويين متساويان، ونقيض الأعم أخص من نقيض الأخص، فإذا جعل نقيض الموضوع محمولا، ونقيض المحمول موضوعا وجب أن يصدق مطلقة عامة؛ لكون المحمول حينئذ مساويا أو أعم، فثبت عكس الخمسة منها. وحله أن المراد بالنقيضين ههنا المفهومان المختلفان سلبا وثبوتا، من غير اعتبار التنافي في الجهة، سواء اجتمعا في موضوع واحد أو لا، «كل (ج) (ب) بالضرورة أو دائما أو بالضرورة بشرط (ج) أو دائما ما دام (ج)» لصدق «بعض (لاب) ليس (لاج) بالإمكان أو بالإطلاق أو حين هو (لاب)»، ويلزمه «بعض (لاب) (ج) بإحدى الجهات»، وتنعكس استقامة إلى «بعض (ج) (لاب) بإحدى الجهات»، وهو مناقض للأصل، أو نضمه مع الأصل ونقول: «بعض (لاب) (ج) بإحدى الجهات، وكل (ج) (ب) بإحدى الجهات الأصل، فينتج بعض (لاب) (ب) بالضرورة أو دائما أو حين هو (لاب)»؛ بناء على أن الممكنة المناقضة للضرورية بالمعنى الأعم ينتج في صغرى الشكل الأول. واعترض عليه المتأخرون بمنع لزوم «بعض (لاب) (ج)» لـ«بعض (لاب) ليس (لاج)»؛ فإن الموجبة المحصلة أخص من السالبة المعدولة، والأخص لا يلزم Section on contrapositive. There, the explanation here is like the explanation there. The detail: that the seven universal affirmative propositions whose negatives do not convert directly do not convert by this contrapositive; because 'Every moon is not eclipsed by timing' is true, and 'Every eclipsed thing is non-moon' is false. And here there is a doubt: that the affirmative actual propositions—their predicates are either equal to their subjects or more general; and the negations of equals are equal, and the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. So if the negation of the subject is made predicate, and the negation of the predicate is made subject, it must be that a universal unrestricted proposition is true; because then the predicate is equal or more general. Thus the contrapositive of five of them is established. Its solution: that by the two negations here is meant the two concepts differing by negation and affirmation, without considering incompatibility in modality, whether they are combined in one subject or not; and by them there – i.e., in the chapter of relatives – is meant the two concepts differing and contradictory in modality, such that they cannot both be true of one subject. So in actual propositions, the negation of the predicate by the second consideration is 'Not B always or by temporal possibility', and the subject of the contrapositive is not that; for the Sheikh considered its truth about individuals actually, and al-Farabi by essential possibility. And if taken by the first consideration, we do not grant that the negation of the subject equals the negation of the predicate, and its being more specific than it is only in the negation by the second consideration, so understand. And knowledge is with the Knower of the unseen. And the two perpetuals convert like themselves if necessity is taken in the broader sense. And the non-perpetual and the two general are general customary; for if 'Every not-B is not-C necessarily or always or as long as it is not-B' together with 'Every C is B necessarily or always or necessarily with the condition of C or always as long as C' were not true, then 'Some not-B is not-C possibly or absolutely or when it is not-B' would be true, and it would entail 'Some not-B is C in one of the modes', and it would convert directly to 'Some C is not-B in one of the modes', and that contradicts the premise, or we combine it with the premise and say: 'Some not-B is C in one of the modes, and every C is B in one of the modes (the premise), so it yields that some not-B is B necessarily or always or when it is not-B'; based on that the possible contradictory to the necessary in the broader sense yields in the minor of the first figure. And the later scholars objected by denying the entailment of 'Some not-B is C' from 'Some not-B is not-C'; for the determinate affirmative is more specific than the qualified negative, and the more specific does not entail the more general. And this is what prompted them to change the definition of conversion, and it cannot be answered except by specifying the claim, as passed in the [chapter on] relatives. And the two specific [propositions] convert as general customary restricted by non-perpetuity in some; as for the general customary, because it is necessary for the general, and as for the non-perpetuity in some, because the non-perpetuity of the premise is negative, and it converts as particular. And if you reflect on our saying 'Every writer is moving as long as he is a writer, not always', you would find the universal non-perpetuity in the contrapositive false. And partial affirmatives – only the two specific ones convert from them; for they convert as specific customary. As for their conversion, because C and B are concomitant in a self, let it be D by virtue of the first part, and B is not true of it, so not-B is true of it, and it is impossible that it be established in the when-aspect of C, otherwise B would not be as long as C, rather not-C is established, so that self is D that is not-C as long as it is not-B. Also, it was B, so it is C, so not-C is not always for it.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage discusses the contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) of categorical propositions, a type of immediate inference. The author begins by noting that the seven universal affirmative propositions whose negatives do not convert directly also do not convert by contrapositive, giving the example 'Every moon is not eclipsed by timing' (true) but 'Every eclipsed thing is not a moon' (false). A doubt is raised: since in actual affirmative propositions the predicate is either equal to or broader than the subject, and the negations of equals are equal, and the negation of a broader term is narrower, making the negation of the subject the predicate and the negation of the predicate the subject should yield a true universal proposition, thus establishing contrapositive for five of the seven. The solution distinguishes two meanings of 'negation' (naqīḍ): here (in the chapter of conversion) it means two concepts differing by negation and affirmation without considering modal incompatibility, whereas there (in the chapter of relatives) it means contradictory concepts that cannot be both true of one subject. So for actual propositions, the negation of the predicate by the second meaning is 'Not B always or by temporal possibility', and the subject of the contrapositive is not taken that way; the Shaykh (al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs?) considered actual truth, while al-Fārābī considered essential possibility. If taken by the first meaning, we do not grant the equality. Next, the two perpetual propositions convert like themselves if necessity is taken broadly. The non-perpetual and the two general (ʿāmmatān) convert as general customary, because if the contrapositive were false, it would lead to a contradiction. A later objection is presented: the entailment from 'Some not-B is not-C' to 'Some not-B is C' is denied because a determinate affirmative is more specific than a qualified negative, and the more specific does not entail the more general; this led later scholars to change the definition of conversion, and the answer is to specify the claim. Finally, the two specific propositions (khāṣṣatān) convert as general customary restricted by non-perpetuity in part, and partial affirmatives only the specific ones convert. The reasoning for the conversion of specific propositions is given: if C and B are concomitant in a self D, and B is not true of D, then not-B is true, and if C were true while D is not-B, then B would not hold as long as C, so not-C is true, establishing the contrapositive. Throughout, examples use logical variables (ج, ب, أ, د) typical of Arabic logic.

النقيضان
الفعلية

Contrapositive of Modal Propositions
Application of contrapositive to modal proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a modal categorical proposition (perpetual, non-perpetual, general customary, specific customary) yields a valid contrapositive and what form it takes.

Core Idea
Not all modal propositions convert by contrapositive; each type has its own conditions.

After resolving the doubt for actual propositions, the passage proceeds to examine the contrapositive of modal propositions classified as perpetual (dāʾimatān), non-perpetual (lā dāʾima), general customary (ʿāmmatān ʿurfiyya), and specific customary (khāṣṣatān). Each behaves differently, and the author provides reasoning involving contradictions and logical consequences. Let's break down each type.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two perpetuals
The passage says the two perpetuals convert like themselves if necessity is taken in the broader sense. So 'Every A is B perpetually' has a contrapositive 'Every not-B is not-A perpetually' under broad necessity.
2
Analyze the non-perpetual and two general customary
These convert as general customary. The author gives a proof by contradiction: if the contrapositive were false, it would lead to 'Some not-B is C' and then 'Some not-B is B', which is impossible. This uses a first-figure syllogism.
This proof relies on the premise that the possible contradictory to the necessary yields a conclusion in the first figure.
3
Examine the later objection
Later scholars objected that from 'Some not-B is not-C' we cannot infer 'Some not-B is C' because the determinate affirmative is more specific than the qualified negative. This led them to change the definition of conversion.
The author notes the only answer is to specify the claim, as done in the chapter on relatives.
4
Handle the two specific (khāṣṣatān)
The two specific propositions convert as specific customary, restricted by non-perpetuity in part. The reasoning: if C and B are concomitant in a self D, and B is not true of D, then not-B is true, leading to the contrapositive.
The author concludes with an example: 'Every writer is moving as long as he is a writer, not always' – the contrapositive cannot be universal non-perpetuity.

اللادوام في البعض فلأن لادوام الأصل سالبة، وهي تنعكس جزئية، ولو تدبرت في قولنا «كل كاتب متحرك ما دام كاتبا لا دائما» لوجدت اللادوام الكلي في العكس كاذبا، والموجبات الجزئية لا تنعكس منها إلا الخاصتان ؛ Section on contrapositive. There, the explanation here is like the explanation there. The detail: that the seven universal affirmative propositions whose negatives do not convert directly do not convert by this contrapositive; because 'Every moon is not eclipsed by timing' is true, and 'Every eclipsed thing is non-moon' is false. And here there is a doubt: that the affirmative actual propositions—their predicates are either equal to their subjects or more general; and the negations of equals are equal, and the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. So if the negation of the subject is made predicate, and the negation of the predicate is made subject, it must be that a universal unrestricted proposition is true; because then the predicate is equal or more general. Thus the contrapositive of five of them is established. Its solution: that by the two negations here is meant the two concepts differing by negation and affirmation, without considering incompatibility in modality, whether they are combined in one subject or not; and by them there – i.e., in the chapter of relatives – is meant the two concepts differing and contradictory in modality, such that they cannot both be true of one subject. So in actual propositions, the negation of the predicate by the second consideration is 'Not B always or by temporal possibility', and the subject of the contrapositive is not that; for the Sheikh considered its truth about individuals actually, and al-Farabi by essential possibility. And if taken by the first consideration, we do not grant that the negation of the subject equals the negation of the predicate, and its being more specific than it is only in the negation by the second consideration, so understand. And knowledge is with the Knower of the unseen. And the two perpetuals convert like themselves if necessity is taken in the broader sense. And the non-perpetual and the two general are general customary; for if 'Every not-B is not-C necessarily or always or as long as it is not-B' together with 'Every C is B necessarily or always or necessarily with the condition of C or always as long as C' were not true, then 'Some not-B is not-C possibly or absolutely or when it is not-B' would be true, and it would entail 'Some not-B is C in one of the modes', and it would convert directly to 'Some C is not-B in one of the modes', and that contradicts the premise, or we combine it with the premise and say: 'Some not-B is C in one of the modes, and every C is B in one of the modes (the premise), so it yields that some not-B is B necessarily or always or when it is not-B'; based on that the possible contradictory to the necessary in the broader sense yields in the minor of the first figure. And the later scholars objected by denying the entailment of 'Some not-B is C' from 'Some not-B is not-C'; for the determinate affirmative is more specific than the qualified negative, and the more specific does not entail the more general. And this is what prompted them to change the definition of conversion, and it cannot be answered except by specifying the claim, as passed in the [chapter on] relatives. And the two specific [propositions] convert as general customary restricted by non-perpetuity in some; as for the general customary, because it is necessary for the general, and as for the non-perpetuity in some, because the non-perpetuity of the premise is negative, and it converts as particular. And if you reflect on our saying 'Every writer is moving as long as he is a writer, not always', you would find the universal non-perpetuity in the contrapositive false. And partial affirmatives – only the two specific ones convert from them; for they convert as specific customary. As for their conversion, because C and B are concomitant in a self, let it be D by virtue of the first part, and B is not true of it, so not-B is true of it, and it is impossible that it be established in the when-aspect of C, otherwise B would not be as long as C, rather not-C is established, so that self is D that is not-C as long as it is not-B. Also, it was B, so it is C, so not-C is not always for it.

Commentary
This passage discusses the contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) of categorical propositions, a type of immediate inference. The author begins by noting that the seven universal affirmative propositions whose negatives do not convert directly also do not convert by contrapositive, giving the example 'Every moon is not eclipsed by timing' (true) but 'Every eclipsed thing is not a moon' (false). A doubt is raised: since in actual affirmative propositions the predicate is either equal to or broader than the subject, and the negations of equals are equal, and the negation of a broader term is narrower, making the negation of the subject the predicate and the negation of the predicate the subject should yield a true universal proposition, thus establishing contrapositive for five of the seven. The solution distinguishes two meanings of 'negation' (naqīḍ): here (in the chapter of conversion) it means two concepts differing by negation and affirmation without considering modal incompatibility, whereas there (in the chapter of relatives) it means contradictory concepts that cannot be both true of one subject. So for actual propositions, the negation of the predicate by the second meaning is 'Not B always or by temporal possibility', and the subject of the contrapositive is not taken that way; the Shaykh (al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs?) considered actual truth, while al-Fārābī considered essential possibility. If taken by the first meaning, we do not grant the equality. Next, the two perpetual propositions convert like themselves if necessity is taken broadly. The non-perpetual and the two general (ʿāmmatān) convert as general customary, because if the contrapositive were false, it would lead to a contradiction. A later objection is presented: the entailment from 'Some not-B is not-C' to 'Some not-B is C' is denied because a determinate affirmative is more specific than a qualified negative, and the more specific does not entail the more general; this led later scholars to change the definition of conversion, and the answer is to specify the claim. Finally, the two specific propositions (khāṣṣatān) convert as general customary restricted by non-perpetuity in part, and partial affirmatives only the specific ones convert. The reasoning for the conversion of specific propositions is given: if C and B are concomitant in a self D, and B is not true of D, then not-B is true, and if C were true while D is not-B, then B would not hold as long as C, so not-C is true, establishing the contrapositive. Throughout, examples use logical variables (ج, ب, أ, د) typical of Arabic logic.

Why the Particular Affirmative Does Not Follow from the Particular Negative in Conversion
Author explains conversion of particular negatives and the objection of later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the simple particular affirmative does not entail from a modified particular negative and identify how this objection reshaped the definition of conversion.

Core Idea
The general does not entail the specific: a modified negative is broader than a simple affirmative.

When converting propositions, later logicians discovered a subtle flaw: from 'some non-B is not non-C' (a modified negative), one cannot validly infer 'some non-B is C' (a simple affirmative) because the latter is more specific. This lesson unpacks this logical asymmetry and shows how it forced a refinement in the rule of conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
From the passage, locate the original proposition (some non-B is not non-C) and the purported inference (some non-B is C). Note that the first is a modified negative (predicate negated), the second a simple affirmative.
2
Distinguish simple affirmative from modified negative
A simple affirmative (al-mūjaba al-muḥaṣṣala) has no negation in subject or predicate, while a modified negative (al-sāliba al-maʿdūla) negates the predicate. The modified negative is logically more general because it allows for both the case where the subject is not the predicate and the case where the subject is the negation of the predicate.
For example, 'some non-B is not non-C' includes cases where 'non-B' is C and where 'non-B' is not C (since it is not non-C). The affirmative 'some non-B is C' only covers the first case.
3
Apply the rule: the specific does not follow from the general
Since the simple affirmative is a subset of the modified negative, inferring the affirmative from the negative would be like deducing a specific case from a general one, which is invalid without additional premises.
This is the core of the later scholars' objection.
4
Understand the consequence for the definition of conversion
Because of this objection, later scholars modified the definition of conversion to exclude such invalid inferences. The passage notes that the only answer is to restrict the claim, as earlier discussed in the section on relations.
This shows how logical theory evolves through critical refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بالضرورة بشرط (ج) أو دائما ما دام (ج)» لصدق «بعض (لاب) ليس (لاج) بالإمكان أو بالإطلاق أو حين هو (لاب)»، ويلزمه «بعض (لاب) (ج) بإحدى الجهات»، وتنعكس استقامة إلى «بعض (ج) (لاب) بإحدى الجهات»، وهو مناقض للأصل، أو نضمه مع الأصل ونقول: «بعض (لاب) (ج) بإحدى الجهات، وكل (ج) (ب) بإحدى الجهات الأصل، فينتج بعض (لاب) (ب) بالضرورة أو دائما أو حين هو (لاب)»؛ بناء على أن الممكنة المناقضة للضرورية بالمعنى الأعم ينتج في صغرى الشكل الأزل. واعترض عليه المتأخرون بمنع لزوم «بعض (لاب) (ج)» لـ«بعض لاب) ليس (لاج)»؛ فإن الموجبة المحصلة أخص من السالبة المعدولة، والأخص لا يلزم الأعم، وهذا هو الباعث لهم على تغيير تعريف العكس، ولا يمكن جوابه إلا بتخصيص الدعوى، كما مر في النسب.
Or with necessity condition (C) or always as long as (C) is true of (some non-B is not non-C) by possibility or absolutely or when it is (non-B), and it entails (some non-B is C) in one of the modes, and it converts directly to (some C is non-B) in one of the modes, and that contradicts the original. Or we combine it with the original and say: (some non-B is C) in one of the modes, and (every C is B) in one of the modes from the original, so it yields (some non-B is B) necessarily or always or when it is (non-B), based on the fact that the possible contradictory to the necessary in the broader sense yields in the minor premise of the first figure. And the later scholars objected, preventing the entailment of (some non-B is C) from (some non-B is not non-C); for the simple affirmative is more specific than the modified negative, and the more specific does not entail the more general. This is what prompted them to change the definition of conversion, and there is no answer except by restricting the claim, as previously mentioned in the discussion of relations.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage discusses the immediate inference of conversion (عكس) for modal propositions, particularly the conversion of a particular negative (some non-B is not non-C) into a particular affirmative (some non-B is C) and then to (some C is non-B). The author notes that this contradicts the original premise, so they combine it with the original to produce a conclusion in the first figure. Later scholars objected to the step from the particular negative to the particular affirmative, arguing that the simple affirmative (some non-B is C) is more specific (اخص) than the modified negative (some non-B is not non-C), and the more specific does not necessarily follow from the more general. This objection led them to redefine the rule of conversion. The author concludes that the only response is to restrict the claim, as previously discussed in the section on relations (النسب). The text illustrates a critical refinement in logical theory, showing how subtle distinctions in proposition types affect valid inference.
