Conversion of Special Particular Propositions
Author defines conversion rule for two special propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which particular affirmative propositions convert and describe the restrictions on their conversion.

Core Idea
Only two temporally conditioned particular affirmatives convert, and only to a common conditional with non-permanence.

This passage explains a nuanced conversion rule in modal logic. While beginners assume all particular affirmatives convert, the author shows that only those with a temporal condition (e.g., 'some A is B as long as it is A') actually convert, and the converse is not universally permanent. You will learn to parse the technical terms and derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special propositions
The term 'الخاصتان' refers to particular affirmative propositions with a temporal condition, like 'some A is B as long as it is A'. Recognize their modality: they involve duration (as long as).
In standard texts, 'khassa' might mean essential property, but here it is a technical modal term.
2
Understand the conversion result
The text says these convert to 'عرفية عامة' (common conditional) but with a restriction: 'مقيدة باللادوام في البعض' (restricted by non-permanence in some). This means the converse is not universally true for all individuals.
Common conditional is a proposition that holds as long as the subject exists, but not necessarily always.
3
Analyze the restriction: non-permanence
The non-permanence (اللادوام) of the original is a negative modal (e.g., 'not always'). In conversion, it becomes a particular proposition ('in some'). The author illustrates with the example: 'كل كاتب متحرك ما دام كاتبا لا دائما' – Here the universal non-permanence in the converse would be false.
The restriction ensures logical consistency.
4
Follow the derivation with variables
The author uses letters ج, ب, د to show the conversion. If C and B are coextensive in an essence D, then from 'D is B as long as C' we derive 'D is non-C as long as non-B'. This shows the logical mechanism.
This is a typical immediate inference in modal logic.
5
Confirm the exception: only these two convert
The passage stresses that from particular affirmatives, only the special ones (الخاصتان) convert. This is a key exception to remember.
Other particular affirmatives (e.g., 'some men are writers') do not convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخاصتان تنعكسان عرفية عامة مقيدة باللادوام في البعض، أما العرفية العامة فلكونها لازمة للعام، وأما اللادوام في البعض فلأن لادوام الأصل سالبة، وهي تنعكس جزئية، ولو تدبرت في قولنا كل كاتب متحرك ما دام كاتبا لا دائما لوجدت اللادوام الكلي في العكس كاذبا، والموجبات الجزئية لا تنعكس منها إلا الخاصتان؛ فإنهما تنعكسان عرفية خاصة، أما انعكاسهما فلأن (ج) و(ب) تلازما في ذات، وليكن (د) بحكم الجزء الأول، ولم يصدق عليه (ب)، فيصدق عليه (لاب)، ومحال أن يثبت في الحينية (ج)، وإلا لما كان (ب) ما دام (ج)، بل يثبت (لاج)، فتلك الذات، هي (د) (لاج) ما دام (لاب). وأيضا قد كان (ب) ، فهو (ج) ، فليس (لاج) دائما له.
And the two special propositions convert to a common conditional restricted by non-permanence in some. As for the common conditional, it is because it is necessary for the general. As for the non-permanence in some, it is because the non-permanence of the original is a negative, and it converts as a particular. If you ponder our statement 'Every writer is moving as long as he is a writer, not always', you would find the universal non-permanence in the converse is false. And from the particular affirmatives, only the two special ones convert; for they convert to a specific conditional. As for their conversion, it is because (C) and (B) are coextensive in an essence, so let it be (D) by the ruling of the first part, and (B) is not true of it, so (non-B) is true of it, and it is impossible that (C) is affirmed in the temporary, otherwise it would not be (B) as long as (C), rather (non-C) is affirmed, so that essence is (D) that is (non-C) as long as (non-B). Also, it was (B), so it is (C), so (non-C) is not always true of it.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rule for what the author calls 'الخاصتان' (the two special propositions). These are particular affirmative propositions with a temporal condition (e.g., 'some A is B as long as it is A'). The author states that these two convert to common conditional propositions (عرفية عامة) but with a restriction: the converse is not universal in terms of non-permanence (اللادوام). The general rule is that from particular affirmatives, only these two convert, and they convert to a specific conditional (عرفية خاصة). The explanation uses a technical example with variables (ج, ب, د) to show how the truth conditions work: if C and B are coextensive in an essence D, then from 'D is B as long as C' we derive that D is non-C as long as non-B. The passage demonstrates the careful modal logic of conversion in the Islamic tradition, emphasizing that not all particulars convert; only those with specific temporal modalities do.

Key Terms
الخاصتان
العرفية العامة
اللادوام

A) عرفية خاصة (specific conditional)
B) عرفية عامة (common conditional)
C) دائمة (permanent)
D) ممكنة (possible)

Key Takeaway
Only the two special temporally conditioned particular affirmatives convert to common conditional, and the converse carries a non-permanence restriction.

Contraposition of Particular Affirmatives: The Dispute with Ibn Sina
Author presents the rule and dispute on particular affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why particular affirmatives do not universally contraposit and identify Ibn Sina's error using counterexamples.

Core Idea
Not all particular affirmatives contraposit; the predicate may be necessary for the subject's complement.

Contraposition is a logical operation that swaps and negates terms, but its application to particular affirmatives is limited. In this lesson, we examine the author's counterexample to Ibn Sina's claim that all particular affirmatives contraposit, using the examples of 'animal' and 'human' to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and counterexample
The author states that 'some non-B is non-C' is true conditionally. He then gives the example: 'some animal is non-human' is true, but its supposed contrapositive 'some human is non-animal' is false, showing that not all particular affirmatives contraposit.
The example uses simple propositions: animal/human relation.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's argument
Ibn Sina argued that there is always some existent or non-existent devoid of both C and B, so 'some non-B is non-C' follows. Trace his reasoning: if something exists that is neither C nor B, then it is non-B and non-C, so the contrapositive holds.
This would imply all particular affirmatives contraposit, which the author refutes.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author refutes by noting that C may be necessary for non-B, so nothing can be devoid of both. In the example, non-human (non-C) implies animal (B)? Actually, 'human' implies 'animal', so something non-human can still be animal. But the point: if C is necessary for non-B, then any non-B is C, so nothing is both non-B and non-C. This blocks Ibn Sina's argument.
The example: 'some animal is non-human' is true, but its contrapositive 'some human is non-animal' is false because human implies animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في عكس النقيض

والجواب من الجائز أن يكون (ج) لازما لـ (لاب)، فلا يمكن خلو شيء ء عن (ج) و(ب) ، كما في المثال المضروب، وهذا غريب من مثله. والسوالب كلية كانت أو جزئية تنعكس جزئية، فالدائمتان والعامتان تنعكس حينية مطلقة ، والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والمطلقة العامة مطلقة عامة، والممكنتان ممكنة عامة، إن أخذ الإمكان مقابلا للضرورة العامة ؛ إذ لو لم يصدق «بعض (لاب) ليس (لاج) حين هو (لاب) أو بالإطلاق أو بالإمكان»، مع لا شيء من (ج) ب) أو بعضه ليس (ب) بإحدى الجهات المذكورة» لصدق كل (لاب) (لاج) ما دام (لاب) أو دائما أو بالضرورة ، وتنعكس بعكس النقيض إلى كل (ج) (ب) ما دام (ج) أو دائما أو بالضرورة»، وهي مناقضة للأصل.
والخاصتان تنعكسان حينية لا دائمة .
أما الحينية فلأنها لازمة للأعم، وأما اللادوام فلأنه لو لم يكن (لاج) بالفعل كان (ج) دائما، فهو ليس (ب) دائما ؛ لأنه كان في الجزء الأول من الأصل ليس (ب) ما دام (ج)، وهو مناف للادوام الأصل. وأما الشرطية
Section on contraposition
So (some non-B is non-C) is true, as long as (non-B) not always. As for the non-conversion of the rest, it is because (some animal is non-human) is true in one of the modes of simple propositions, and (some moon is non-eclipsed) in one of the modes of composite propositions, aside from the mode of the two special ones, while (some human is non-animal) and (some eclipsed is non-moon) are false. And the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) disagreed with this, and said: all particular affirmatives convert by this conversion, and he argued that something from existents or non-existents is devoid of (C) and (B), so (some non-B is non-C). If this were sound, it would also indicate the conversion of the seven universal affirmatives, but to the particular. The answer is that it is possible that (C) is necessary for (non-B), so it is impossible for something to be devoid of (C) and (B), as in the example given, and this is strange from him. And the negatives, whether universal or particular, convert as particular. So the two permanent and the two common convert to absolute temporal; the two temporal and the two existential and the absolute common convert to absolute common; the two possible convert to common possible, if possibility is taken as opposite to general necessity. For if (some non-B is not non-C) when it is (non-B) or absolutely or by possibility were not true, along with (no C is B) or (some C is not B) in the mentioned modes, then (every non-B is non-C) as long as it is (non-B) or always or necessarily would be true, and it would convert by contraposition to (every C is B) as long as it is (C) or always or necessarily, and that contradicts the original. And the two special ones convert to temporal not permanent. As for the temporal, it is because it is necessary for the general. As for the non-permanence, it is because if it were not (non-C) actually, then (C) would be always true, so it is not (B) always, because it was in the first part of the original not (B) as long as (C), and that contradicts the non-permanence of the original. And as for the conditional...
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This section (فصل) is devoted to the logical operation of contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author begins by giving an example: 'some non-B is non-C' is true as long as it is non-B, but not always. He then explains why other particular affirmatives do not convert, using examples from simple and composite propositions: 'some animal is non-human' (simple) is true, but 'some human is non-animal' is false; 'some moon is non-eclipsed' (composite) is true, but 'some eclipsed is non-moon' is false. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) disagreed, claiming that all particular affirmatives convert via this contraposition, arguing that there is always something devoid of both C and B, which yields 'some non-B is non-C'. The author refutes this by noting that C may be necessary for non-B, so nothing can be devoid of both, as in the example. Then the author provides the systematic rules: all negatives (universal or particular) contraposit to particular; the two permanent and two common negatives convert to absolute temporal; the two temporal, two existential, and absolute common convert to absolute common; the two possible convert to common possible, provided possibility is taken as opposite to general necessity. The justification uses a conditional argument: if the contrapositive were false, the original would lead to a contradiction. Finally, the two special ones (الخاصتان) convert to temporal but not permanent; the temporal aspect is because it is necessary for the general, and the non-permanence is argued through a hypothetical. The passage is a comprehensive treatment of contraposition across modal and temporal categories.

Particular affirmatives do not universally contraposit because the predicate may be necessary for the complement of the subject, as shown in the animal-human example.

Rules for Negative Propositions in Contraposition
Author details the rules of contraposition for all negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contrapositive of any negative categorical proposition and its modal modification according to the author's rules.

Core Idea
All negatives contraposit to particular negatives, but the modality of the conclusion is determined by the modality of the premise.

After establishing the limitations for particular affirmatives, the author provides a systematic treatment of negative propositions. He categorizes negatives by modal type and specifies the modal strength of the contrapositive. Understanding these rules is essential for correct logical reasoning in modal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of negative propositions
The author lists modal types: the two permanent (دائمتان) and the two common (عامتان), the two temporal (وقتيتان), the two existential (وجوديتان), the absolute common (مطلقة عامة), and the two possible (ممكنتان). Recognize that these are universal and particular forms of each modal type.
These correspond to the standard modal categories in Aristotelian logic.
2
Match each category to its contrapositive modality
For the two permanent and two common, the contrapositive is absolute temporal (حينية مطلقة). For the two temporal, two existential, and absolute common, it is absolute common (مطلقة عامة). For the two possible, it is common possible (ممكنة عامة) provided possibility is opposed to general necessity.
The author later justifies these with a conditional argument.
3
Understand the justification via conditional reasoning
The author argues that if the contrapositive (e.g., some non-B is not non-C) were false, then every non-B would be non-C, which would contraposit to every C is B, contradicting the original negative proposition. This shows the contrapositive must be true with the specified modality.
The justification relies on the principle of reductio ad absurdum.

Contraposition of the 'Two Special' Propositions (الخاصتان)
Author gives rules for the two special propositions in contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the two special propositions contraposit only to temporal (hiniyya) and not permanent, using the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
The two special propositions are unique: they contraposit to temporal but not permanent due to their relation to the general and the non-permanence condition.

The author concludes the section with the rules for the two special propositions (الخاصتان). These are distinct from the previous categories and have a specific rule: they convert to temporal (hiniyya) not permanent. The author provides a justification involving the necessity of the general and a conditional argument about non-permanence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special propositions
From context, 'الخاصتان' likely refer to the particular affirmative and particular negative propositions that are not covered by the previous modal categories. They are 'special' because they have a unique behavior.
Some commentators identify them as the two particular propositions (موجبة جزئية وسالبة جزئية) in the context of modal logic.
2
Understand why they convert to temporal
The author says: 'As for the temporal, because it is necessary for the general.' This means that the temporal mode is a necessary consequence of the generality of the original proposition. In other words, the contrapositive's truth is tied to the time when the subject is instantiated, not permanently.
The temporal mode is weaker than permanent.
3
Understand why they do not convert to permanent
The author gives a conditional argument: if the contrapositive were permanent, then if the predicate (non-C) were not actually true, C would always be true, leading to a contradiction with the original proposition's non-permanence. This shows that a permanent contrapositive is impossible.
The argument is subtle but relies on the original's modal nature.

For if (some non-B is not non-C) when it is (non-B) or absolutely or by possibility were not true, along with (no C is B) or (some C is not B) in the mentioned modes, then (every non-B is non-C) as long as it is (non-B) or always or necessarily would be true, and it would convert by contraposition to (every C is B) as long as it is (C) or always or necessarily, and that contradicts the original. And the two special ones convert to temporal not permanent. As for the temporal, it is because it is necessary for the general. As for the non-permanence, it is because if it were not (non-C) actually, then (C) would be always true, so it is not (B) always, because it was in the first part of the original not (B) as long as (C), and that contradicts the non-permanence of the original. And as for the conditional...
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This section (فصل) is devoted to the logical operation of contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author begins by giving an example: 'some non-B is non-C' is true as long as it is non-B, but not always. He then explains why other particular affirmatives do not convert, using examples from simple and composite propositions: 'some animal is non-human' (simple) is true, but 'some human is non-animal' is false; 'some moon is non-eclipsed' (composite) is true, but 'some eclipsed is non-moon' is false. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) disagreed, claiming that all particular affirmatives convert via this contraposition, arguing that there is always something devoid of both C and B, which yields 'some non-B is non-C'. The author refutes this by noting that C may be necessary for non-B, so nothing can be devoid of both, as in the example. Then the author provides the systematic rules: all negatives (universal or particular) contraposit to particular; the two permanent and two common negatives convert to absolute temporal; the two temporal, two existential, and absolute common convert to absolute common; the two possible convert to common possible, provided possibility is taken as opposite to general necessity. The justification uses a conditional argument: if the contrapositive were false, the original would lead to a contradiction. Finally, the two special ones (الخاصتان) convert to temporal but not permanent; the temporal aspect is because it is necessary for the general, and the non-permanence is argued through a hypothetical. The passage is a comprehensive treatment of contraposition across modal and temporal categories.

Key Terms
الخاصتان
حينية
لازمة للأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The two special propositions contraposit to temporal (hiniyya) only, not permanent, due to their relation to the general and the conditional argument about non-permanence.

Conversion by Contradiction: Rule and Dispute
Author defines conversion by contradiction and discusses the disagreement over particular affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion by contradiction to categorical propositions and identify conditions where it fails, using the example of al-Shaykh's error.

Core Idea
Conversion by contradiction is not universal; al-Shaykh's claim overextends.

This lesson introduces 'aks al-naqid (conversion by contradiction), an immediate inference that swaps and negates terms. The author states a basic rule and then examines a disagreement with al-Shaykh, who argued that all particular affirmatives convert by this method. By analyzing the examples and the rebuttal, you will learn when this operation is valid and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Understand the operation
Conversion by contradiction transforms a proposition into one where both terms are negated and swapped. For example, from 'Some A is not B' we get 'Some not-B is not-A' (or in the passage, 'Some B is not A' as per the example).
The author's example uses 'Some animal is not human' which converts to 'Some human is not animal'.
2
Identify the condition
The author states that the converted proposition is true as long as the subject term (not-B) is not always true (i.e., not necessary). If it is necessary, the conversion fails.
'Not-B' being not always means its complement is not universal.
3
Test with examples
The author gives 'Some animal is not human' (true) and its conversion 'Some human is not animal' (false), showing the conversion is invalid in this case. 'Some moon is not eclipsed' also fails to convert.
These examples come from modal logic: simple vs composite modalities.
4
Analyze al-Shaykh's argument
Al-Shaykh claimed all particular affirmatives convert by this method. His proof: for any thing, it cannot be free of both C and not-B, so 'Some not-B is not-C' is always true.
He used variables (B, C) to argue universally.
5
Evaluate the author's rebuttal
The author objects that if al-Shaykh's argument held, it would also prove universal affirmatives convert, which is false. Moreover, C might be a necessary concomitant of not-B, making it impossible for anything to be free of both.
This rebuttal highlights the role of modal necessity in conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في عكس النقيض
فصدق بعض (لاب) (لاج) ، ما دام (لاب) لا دائما .
وأما عدم انعكاس البواقي فلصدق بعض الحيوان هو لا إنسان بإحدى جهات البسائط ، وبعض القمر هو لا منخسف بإحدى جهات المركبات ، سوى جهة الخاصتين، مع كذب بعض الإنسان لا حيوان» و«بعض المنخسف لا قمر. وخالف الشيخ في هذا، وقال: الموجبات الجزئية كلها تنعكس بهذا العكس، واستدل بأن شيءا من الموجودات أو المعدومات خالية عن (ج) و (ب)، فبعض (لاب) (لاج)، وهذا لو تم لدل على انعكاس الموجبات الكلية السبعة أيضا، لكن إلى الجزئية .
والجواب من الجائز أن يكون (ج) لازما لـ (لاب)، فلا يمكن خلو شيء عن (ج) و(ب) ، كما في المثال المضروب، وهذا غريب من مثله.
A chapter on conversion by contradiction.
So 'Some of what is not B is not C' is true, as long as 'what is not B' is not always [true].
As for the non-conversion of the remaining [propositions], it is because 'Some animal is not human' is true in one of the simple modalities, and 'Some moon is not eclipsed' in one of the composite modalities except the modality of the two specials, while 'Some human is not animal' and 'Some non-eclipsed is not moon' are false. Al-Shaykh disagreed with this and said: All particular affirmatives convert by this conversion, and he argued that there is no existent or non-existent thing that is free of (C) and (B), so 'Some not-B is not-C' [is true]. If this were sound, it would also prove the conversion of the seven universal affirmatives, but into particular [affirmatives].
The answer is that it is possible for (C) to be a necessary concomitant of (not-B), so it is impossible for anything to be free of both (C) and (B), as in the example given. And this is strange coming from one like him.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on conversion by contradiction ('aks al-naqid), which is a logical operation where a proposition is transformed into another by negating both terms and swapping them. The author first states a rule: 'Some of what is not B is not C' is true, provided that 'not B' is not always true (i.e., the subject term is not necessary). Then he explains why other types do not convert, giving examples from simple and composite modalities. For instance, 'Some animal is not human' is true, but its contradiction 'Some human is not animal' is false. Al-Shaykh, a notable scholar, objects that all particular affirmatives do convert by this method. His argument: for any thing, it cannot be free of both C and not-B, so 'Some not-B is not-C' must be true. The author retorts that this argument would also prove that universal affirmatives convert, which is false. Moreover, the answer is that C might be a necessary concomitant of not-B, so they are not both absent. The author finds this error surprising from al-Shaykh.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
الموجبات الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The predicate C may be a necessary concomitant of not-B, making the conversion invalid.
C) Both A and B are correct.
D) The example of 'Some animal is not human' directly contradicts the claim.

Key Takeaway
Conversion by contradiction is not universally valid; its success depends on whether the predicate is a necessary concomitant of the subject.

Conversion Rule for Negative Propositions
Author details conversion rules for universal and particular negatives, including the two special modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conversion rule for any negative proposition and identify the resulting modal type.

Core Idea
All negatives convert to particular negatives; modality determines the temporal or absolute quality.

In this passage, the author lays down the general rule for converting negative propositions in modal logic. Understanding this rule is crucial for proofs by contradiction in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where negations of rulings must be handled with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type and modality of the original negative
Determine whether the negative is universal ('No A is B') or particular ('Some A is not B'), and note its modality: perpetual, common, time-limited, existential, common absolute, or possible.
The author lists specific modalities; refer back to earlier definitions if needed.
2
Apply the conversion rule
Regardless of the original type, the converted proposition is always a particular negative ('Some B is not A').
This is the first part of the rule: 'negatives, whether universal or particular, convert into particular [negatives]'.
3
Map the modality of the result
Use the author's mapping: perpetual/common → absolute temporal; time-limited/existential/common absolute → common absolute; possible → common possible (if possibility is opposed to universal necessity).
The conversion preserves the 'some' quantifier but transforms the modality according to these rules.
4
Justify using proof by contradiction
The author provides a logical justification: if the converted proposition were false, it would imply a universal affirmative that, upon conversion, contradicts the original. This is a standard immediate inference proof.
This step deepens understanding of why the rule holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسوالب كلية كانت أو جزئية تنعكس جزئية، فالدائمتان والعامتان تنعكس حينية مطلقة ، والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والمطلقة العامة مطلقة عامة، والممكنتان ممكنة عامة، إن أخذ الإمكان مقابلا للضرورة العامة ؛ إذ لو لم يصدق «بعض (لاب) ليس (لاج) حين هو (لاب) أو بالإطلاق أو بالإمكان»، مع لا شيء من (ج) ب) أو بعضه ليس (ب) بإحدى الجهات المذكورة» لصدق كل (لاب) (لاج) ما دام (لاب) أو دائما أو بالضرورة ، وتنعكس بعكس النقيض إلى كل (ج) (ب) ما دام (ج) أو دائما أو بالضرورة»، وهي مناقضة للأصل.
والخاصتان تنعكسان حينية لا دائمة .
أما الحينية فلأنها لازمة للأعم، وأما اللادوام فلأنه لو لم يكن (لاج) بالفعل كان (ج) دائما، فهو ليس (ب) دائما ؛ لأنه كان في الجزء الأول من الأصل ليس (ب) ما دام (ج)، وهو مناف للادوام الأصل.
And negatives, whether universal or particular, convert into particular [negatives]. So the two perpetuals and the two common [negatives] convert into a absolute temporal [negative]; the two time-limited and the two existential and the common absolute convert into common absolute; the two possible convert into common possible, if possibility is taken as opposed to universal necessity. Because if 'Some not-B is not-C at the time it is not-B, or absolutely, or possibly' were not true, while 'No C is B' or 'Some C is not B' in one of the mentioned modalities is [true], then 'Every not-B is C as long as it is not-B, or always, or necessarily' would be true, and it would convert by contradictory conversion into 'Every C is B as long as it is C, or always, or necessarily', which contradicts the original.
And the two special [negatives] convert into temporal [negatives], not perpetual.
As for the temporal [conversion], because it is necessary for the more general. As for non-perpetuity, because if 'not-C' were not actual, then C would be perpetual, so it is not that B is perpetual; because in the first part of the original, it was 'B is not what is C', which conflicts with the non-perpetuity of the original.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage continues the analysis of conversion by contradiction for negative propositions. The author states a general rule: all negatives, whether universal or particular, convert to particular negatives. He then specifies how different modal types convert: perpetual and common negatives convert to absolute temporal negatives; time-limited, existential, and common absolute negatives convert to common absolute; possible negatives convert to common possible (if possibility is opposed to universal necessity). The justification is a proof by contradiction: if the converted proposition were false, then the original would imply a universal affirmative that, when converted, contradicts the original original. Next, the author discusses 'the two special' negatives (likely referring to special absolute or special necessary). They convert to temporal negatives, not perpetual. The reason: temporal conversion is necessary because it is more general; non-perpetuity is shown by considering that if the negation were actual, then the positive would be perpetual, which is inconsistent with the original's non-perpetuity.

Key Terms
السوالب
الخاصتان
الدائمتان
العامتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
All negative propositions, regardless of modality, convert to particular negatives, with the modality determined by a fixed mapping.

The Two Special Negatives and Their Conversion
Author details conversion rules for universal and particular negatives, including the two special modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the conversion of the two special negatives and explain why they yield only temporal negatives.

Core Idea
Special negatives convert to temporal, not perpetual, because the negation of a special modality implies a perpetuity that contradicts the original.

The author introduces a special case: 'the two special negatives' (al-khāṣṣatān). These convert to temporal negatives but not perpetual. The reasoning involves a subtle modal interplay that illustrates the richness of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special negatives
Recall what 'special' means: these are modal negatives with a specific type of necessity or absoluteness (e.g., special necessary negative). The author does not define them here but refers to an earlier classification.
If unsure, consult the definitions of modal propositions in the preceding sections.
2
Apply the conversion rule for specials
The rule: special negatives convert to temporal negatives, not to perpetual ones. This is a restriction beyond the general rule.
Temporal means 'at some time' (called 'ḥīniyya').
3
Understand the justification for temporal
The author says temporal is 'necessary for the more general' – meaning that if the converted proposition is temporal, it covers a wider range of possibilities, so it's safer.
This is a logical maxim: when in doubt, the weaker modality suffices.
4
Understand the justification for non-perpetuity
The author uses a proof: if actually not-C, then C is perpetual; but the original says 'B is not what is C as long as it is C', which conflicts with non-perpetuity. Therefore, the conversion cannot be perpetual.
This is a concise proof by contradiction using the special modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والخاصتان تنعكسان حينية لا دائمة .
أما الحينية فلأنها لازمة للأعم، وأما اللادوام فلأنه لو لم يكن (لاج) بالفعل كان (ج) دائما، فهو ليس (ب) دائما ؛ لأنه كان في الجزء الأول من الأصل ليس (ب) ما دام (ج)، وهو مناف للادوام الأصل.
And negatives, whether universal or particular, convert into particular [negatives]. So the two perpetuals and the two common [negatives] convert into a absolute temporal [negative]; the two time-limited and the two existential and the common absolute convert into common absolute; the two possible convert into common possible, if possibility is taken as opposed to universal necessity. Because if 'Some not-B is not-C at the time it is not-B, or absolutely, or possibly' were not true, while 'No C is B' or 'Some C is not B' in one of the mentioned modalities is [true], then 'Every not-B is C as long as it is not-B, or always, or necessarily' would be true, and it would convert by contradictory conversion into 'Every C is B as long as it is C, or always, or necessarily', which contradicts the original.
And the two special [negatives] convert into temporal [negatives], not perpetual.
